"THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
GEORGE ELIOT AT SEA.
I have read somewhere that George Eliot was “ a
fiery soul.’ Perhaps that is the reason she was
never at home in the venter. I suspect she could
not swim ; and when the characters in her novels
go boating she is always at sea—even if they are
only sculling on a river.
This is a pity, for she drowns heroes and heroine 8
with ag little compunction as if they were kittens.
I complain that they do not drown naturally. One
does not object to Dunstan Cass, in “ Silas
Marner,” walking into the stone-pit to perish;
although you think that he, a hoy bred in the
neighbourhood, should have had more sense. Nor
do I care to dispute about probabilities when
Adam Bede finds his father in the Willow Brook.
But these are simple cases, which it would be
hard to bungle if one tried. It is when she
attempts complex drownings that we feel George
ElioFs deficiencies.
For example, Tito’s death in “ Romola ” is not
well done. A fine swimmer, as he is said to be,
would not get exhausted in a few minutes. In a
river, he Could easily swim five or even ten miles
with the current. Or he could simply drift along.
No swimmer would persevere to exhaustion in
such a case. He would either make for the shore
when he grew tired, or turn on his back and float.
If Tito had lus mail-coat on I could understand
it, but “he no longer wore his armour.” Of
course it is necessary for him to become exhausted,
or he would not fall a prey to Baldassarre. But
machine-gods should confine themselves to the
land, and not meddle with people in the water. I
am a pretty fair swimmer myself, and in the
name of Leander, Byron, . Gormly, Self and Co., I
protest against such unwarrantable catastrophes.
Were Tito alive, he would protest, too. If lady
novelists think a fellow gets exhausted after
swimming a few hundred yards in a river, with
the stream, lady novelists must be taught dif-
ferently.
Then, as to the fate of Tom and Maggie Tulli-
ver, one’s indignation is excusably warm. If Tom
could not manage the boat so as to avoid being
stove in, he should have given the sculls to his
sister. I am positive Maggie would have saved
both lives, in spite of the conduct of the floating
debris, which is truly extraordinary. To begin
with, buildings are not usually broken into “ huge
fragments ”by a flood. If they float away at all,
they nearly always float bodily. Nor do “ huge
fragments make one wide mass across the stream.”
Variations of the current prevent that. And
even supposing that Tom was in the way of a
mass of huge fragments, carried along at a
speed of six miles an hour—a sufficient esti-
mate under the circumstances —how was he
overtaken when his beat was presumably drifting
just as fast? What hindered him from turn-
ing the boat’s head and pulling away with the
current ? Or why did he not endeavour to obtain
footing on a “huge fragment”? Sailors, in a
collision, have leaped upon the bows of an
approaching vessel, or upon an iceberg. The
picture in my copy of “ The Mill on the Floss ”
shows the approach of a “ huge fragment” which
looks like part of the foundation of a crane. One
of Tom’s sculls is gone, and he has stopped pull-
ing. He is standing up in the boat, a light 15ft.
skiff, clasping Maggie in his arms and waiting for
annihilation. What I should do, in his place,
would be to make the skiff fast to a ring-bolt on
the “ huge fragment” (there is one convenient in
the picture) and float downstream looking for
something to turn up. No doubt Maggie would
have done this had not her brother flurried her.
It is provoking to see two young people drowned
so foolishly. Ido not regret Tom much, but
Maggie was a girl in a thousand.
The manner of Grandcourt’s taking-off in
“ Daniel Deronda ”is equally unsatisfactory. I
do not grieve for Grandcourt any more than for
Tom, but he was not a bad fellow at bottom.
There was no cant about him, at all events ; and
Gwendolen is rather worse off when he goes, since
she is torn by remorse, and Deronda will have
nothing to do with her, In any case, the affair is
clumsily managed. I have never sailed a boat in
the Gulf of Genoa, so I do not know what the
wind is like ; but Grandcourt was probably trying
to jibe when a puff came. This would agree with
Gwendolen’s statement: “ I don’t know how it
was —he was turning the sail —there was a gust
—he was struck.” The fishermen thought
maybe he had been “ knocked overboard by the
flapping of the sail while putting about;”
but this is hardly likely. If he carried
a boom, and handled the boat very care-
lessly, he might have been swung overboard,
but where was Gwendolen sitting that she
wasn’t hit too? And, even if the boat jibed,
would Grandcourt be “swept over” without her
capsizing ? Then what is he about to drown so
quickly ? Gwendolen was not sure that he could
swim ; but is it probable that an English country
gentleman like Grandcourt had never learnt?
Would he be likely to take his wife out sailing if
he could not swim ? Deronda thought “he must
have been seized with cramp,” but people do not
get cramp the minute they fall into water; and
even if they do, and can swim, they keep afloat.
Of course I would like to know what kind of boat
Grandcourt hired before pronouncing a decided
opinion; but the accident is certainly mysterious.
I do not go so far as to say that George
Eliot’s works are entirely spoilt by her failure to
appreciate these considerations; but, as a swim-
mer, I would be much better pleased had Tito
floated five or six miles down the river, then
landed fresh as paint and killed Baldassarre after
a brief struggle ; or if Tom Tulliver had swum
ashore on his back with Maggie on his arm. They
had plenty of time to take off any clothes that
encumbered them; and Tom, as a miller’s son,
who had doubtless bathed in the mill-pool
as soon as he could walk, should have been
easily equal to such a feat. Grandcourt
ought to have capsized the boat and sup-
ported Gwendolen in the water till help came,
when her feelings towards him would have under-
gone a complete change, and they would have lived
happily together. Of course, we must take our
author as we find her ; but I am astonished that
Lewes, with all his versatility, did not know
enough to set his wife right. One thing is plain :
we should, for the sake of future lady novelists,
teach all our girls to swim. The only alternative
is to insist that doomed personages be removed by
poison or the cord.
George Eliot’s Hovels.
Complete edition in red cloth, illustrated
and well-printed, £1 19s. 6d. ; or separate
volumes as under :
Adam Bede. Clever and characteristic,
4s. 2d.
Daniel Deronda. Heavy and laboured, but
with a certain impressive strength, Bs. 3d.

4s. 2d.
Middlemarch. The author’s most interesting
book, Bs. 3d.
The Mill on the Floss. Perhaps the most
popular, 4s. 2d.
Romola. The author’s own favourite,
4s. 2d.

Scenes of Clerical Life. First essays in
fiction, 4s. 2d.

‘A GOLDEN SHANTY
Much Distressed,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her Majesty the Queen was much distressed at the ter-
rible news of the wreck of the steamer Kibe.
The noisy urchins scampered round
Amidst the careless crowd :
“ An orful wreck ! Three ’underd drown’d !”
They cried the news aloud.
We bought a paper, paused, and read
With melancholy zest,
Then winked our other eye, and said —
“The Queen will be distressed.”
We felt it coming from the first;
Court flunkeys took the hint,
And promptly got a royal burst
Of sorrow into print.
The cable told the touching tale
Exactly as we guessed
We never knew her grief to fail,
The Queen was much distressed-
In time of war, should British blood
Be shed by niggers mean,
Or when an earthquake, or a flood,
Appears upon the scene ;
Or accidents in mines excite
Some public interest,
Then, posing in a blaze of light.
The Queen is much distressed.
The “ gag ” has charmed our loyal friends
For many foolish years :
They seem to think the monarch spends
Her “ screw ” in gracious tears.
But homeless poor, without regret,
Sink starving to their rest:
These live and die in rags, and yet
The Queen is not distressed.
Dear reader, did your mother weep
Upon your sister’s neck,
And couldn’t your good lady sleep
For thinking of that wreck ?
These women true forgot to grieve,
You cheerfully protest—
Nay, what is more, they don’t believe
The Queen was much distressed.

Bevill, N.S.W. licensed victuallers’ secre-
tary, is the first to authoritatively define the
measure of a nobbier. He says sixteen may be
taken from the ordinary bottle.
Darlinghursfc now holds a love-and-em-
bezzlement-artist who persuaded his best girl to
advance him £9O to buy land whereon to build
the honeymooning humpy. The “old woman”
isn’t dead yet any more than the old Adam.
The Melb. papers are loud in praise of the
performances of certain well-advertised detectives
in re the “great tobacco robberies.” Yet the
matter was sprung by a man in the force “on
approbation,” who gets none of the praise and
little of the .£2OO reward. He found the root and
the clever ones found the tree.
( Again the wicked North and the Sawbath!
This time the godless miners are hiring special
Sunday trains to take them from the dusty Towers
to the pleasant banks of the Burdekin, where
picnics al ireseo eventuate. The Ministers’ Union
petitioned Phi Ip against the special train, but that
Minister declines to interfere—only promising that
the police shall be stationed on the picnic ground
to prevent Sabbath desecration.

Captain William Jackson Barry, the his-
torical Maoriland crank whose fish-stories—espe-
cially the one about his ride on the back of a
whale—are the most amazing things of their kind
in this hemisphere, is reported to have an entirely
new version of the Tichborne mystery. According
to the Captain, Cresswell and the famous Claimant
and the mysterious pauper who was buried at
Geelong are all equally bogus. The real Roger
Tichborne is now living in seclusion in N.S.
Wales, and the Captain, who knew him well when
he was a boy, reckons that he could find him if he
is badly wanted. As it happens, he isn’t wanted,
badly or otherwise. This afflicted generation
couldn’t stand any more Tichborne Claimant.

The Victorian drink statistics show that,
in ’9O, £6,730,430 was- spent in liquor, and 18,407
people were convicted of aggravated drunkenness,
or a total of £365 13s. spent in drink for each
drunk convicted. In ’93, on the other band, the
aggregate liquor-bill was £3,749,577, and the num-
ber of drunks captured by the police was 12,688,
equal to only £295 7s. worth of liquor consumed
for each drunk officially captured. This suggests
three possible explanations. One is that the
country has given up champagne and taken to
beer. Another, that owing to the prevailing
scarcity the police can’t select their drunks as
they used to do, and instead of only taking the
rare and picturesque specimens, have to be satis-
fied with whatever specimens they can get. And
the third possibility is that drink is cheaper, and
that a man who formerly required £365 13s. worth
of liquor to make him tight, can now get
gloriously fuddled for the comparatively small
outlay of £295 7s.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling —with postage aided where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
*Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS, to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid far same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to he re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, SI Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal '.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— lt. A. Thompson and Co., S Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland
and South Australia.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription t s
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

Coloured wrapper denotes expiration of subscription.

MAOBILAND, TASMANIA and WES
A USTBALIA.
Owing to Governments of the above-named provinces
having imposed Half-penny postage on all papers from
Ist July, 189 U, agents are informed from that date the

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 23, 1895.
Bung, the Poisoner.
Within the last few weeks New South Wales
has suffered an unpleasant awakening in re
the mysteries of the drink-traffic. The
awakening has had no sort of connection
with teetotalism, and has not served to bring
the Cold Tea millennium one whit nearer.
The public is already aware that water, even
allegedly “pure ” water, contains germs, and
mineral salts, and traces of decayed vegeta-
tion, and dead horse, and old, defunct dog,
and deceased ’possum, and native-bear that
has joined the great majority, therefore the
rampant apostle who demands that the world
shall drink nothing save cold water does not
appeal strongly to the clean-living section of
the community. It knows also that tea is a
strange substance which may have been trod-
den on by leprous feet and packed by scaly
hands, and which is eminently calculated to
arouse suspicion. It has learned that milk is a
substance which is very often squeezed by a
dirty man out of a dirty, tuberculosed cow ;
that the milk, even when it isn’t exposed to
the atmosphere in an unclean dairy, and put
in an unwashed can, and deteriorated through
the cow getting its hind foot in the pail, is a
substance which gathers infection almost
faster than any other ; and, therefore, it has
begun to view this fluid also with a distrust-
ful eye. Also it has learned, at different
times, strange and objectionable facts about
almost everything else which it eats and
drinks. And lately the people of Australia
have ascertained that their various alcoholic
beverages are as unwholesome and as badly
adulterated as the rest, and that art is just
about as unclean and as objectionable as
nature. This discovery doesn’t help on the
temperance cause, for the fact that whisky
is poisonous doesn’t make milk any less
poisonous, and the fact that beer is dirty
doesn’t make tea, or the Chinaman by whom
it is packed, any cleaner. But it creates a
feeling of vague unrest, and a shadowy idea
that something not very well defined should
be done to alleviate the gigantic evil.
* • •
So far as can be gathered from the evi-
dence available, the drink trade is a vast and
complicated system of thievery, adulteration,
dirt and slow poison from beginning to end.
The process begins in Europe, and its ending
is in the back-blocks cemetery where the
victim of the last of a long series of adultera-
tors is buried after his final orgie. Much of
the alleged brandy and whisky imported into
Australia is a ghoulsome compound known
as “ Belgian spirit,” which can be landed in
Sydney —so several experts declare upon
oath—at three shillings per case of two gallons.
The price is not much higher than that of
road-scrapings or any other form of refuse,
and the quality strictly corresponds with the
price. A German horror of about the same
character is another prominent feature in
the importations. A very large item—pro-
bably as large as both the above combined—
is the white spirit of illicit local manufacture,
which is secretly turned out in enormous
quantities and sold at Bs., or less, per gallon,
a figure which fairly corresponds with the
villainousness of its general character. The
amount of thisawfulcompound which is puton
the market cannot be ascertained, but some
idea can be formed of the extent of the trade
by the constant seizures made by the police.
Considering that the discovery of an illicit-
still, conducted in a suitable spot by a reason-
ably intelligent law-breaker, is about as diffi-
cult as the discovery of the long-lost bones
of Leichhardt, the capture, within a small
radius, of eight distillers inside of three
months probably represents many scores
more distillers who are still at large and
undetected.
#
Another branch of the trade is represented
oy the large importations of methylated
spirit, which is supposed, by a stupid fiction
of the law, to be unfit and unavailable for
human consumption, but which can be
rendered drinkable, after a fashion, by a
very simple process well-known to almost
everybody except the legal authorities. This
represents another large item in the spirit
supply of Australia, and accounts for the fact
that the man who buys a glass of Sydney
public-house spirits often has a hazy sort of
idea that he tastes and smells paint in his
glass. Another great feature of the trade is
the alleged whisky and brandy manufactured
by chemists. The lawful stock-in-trade of
the chemist includes everything required for
the cheap compounding of villainous alcoholic
drinks which, in the matter of aroma, taste
and general appearance, can pass inspec-
tion by nine out of ten among ordinary
drinkers. A still larger item is the raw
white spirit “ doctored ” by the publican on
his own premises. This is done so openly
that a book was recently published and circu-
lated, in Sydney, giving full instructions how
to compound out of all manner of cheap and
loathsome ingredients, the beverages which
are matured under the publican’s bed instead
of beneath the sunshine of Burgundy or in
the fogs of Scotland. This volume was
apparently issued under the auspices of a
leading Sydney trader. Further, there is
no security to be gained by buying the
most reputable brands in sealed bottles,
with the label and capsule as a guarantee
of reliability, partly because cheap and
horrible forgeries of every well-known
brand can be imported from Germany or
elsewhere, and chiefly because every pub-
lican, importer, and wholesale-dealer can
buy the labels and capsules in any quan-
tity, and make all his own Hennessy and
Martell and fine old Highland whisky on
the premises.
*
Therefore, the chances are about 100 to 1
against any purchaser being able to get a
single drink of reasonably pure liquor in
N.S. Wales. Even if the average importer
wants to keep faith with the public—
which he doesn’t, because if he does keep
faith with the public he is undersold by un-
scrupulous competitors until he is driven out
of the trade—he is always liable to be
deceived by the German, or Belgian, or
British, or other swindler at the European
end of the trade. If the wholesale dealer
wants to act “on the square”—which he
mostly doesn’t either, for exactly the same
reason—he is deceived, either by the im-
porter or the European manufacturer. If
the retailer desires to be honest—and he
can’t afford to have any such desire, even
assuming that he has a natural tendency
towards honesty, which is wholly improbable
—he is defrauded by the wholesale dealer
and the other two. But, as none of them
has any natural leanings towards honesty, the
importer introduces Belgian spirit at Is. 6d.
a gallon, and the next middleman “blends”
it with other horrors ; and the retailer buys
a certain quantity of it to give his business a
feeble outward pretence of honesty, and
obtains the rest from the chemist, or the
illicit potato-spirit fiend, or else makes it
himself out of minerals, acids, tobacco,
vitriol, aniline dyes, and other beautiful in-
gredients. And the law, as at present con-
structed, in the rum-founded land of New
South Wales at all events, is unable to stop
the sale of books containing recipes for the
manufacture of bogus whisky ; it has no
power to examine or otherwise interfere with
the bogus imported spirit in bond, or to pre-
vent it going into circulation ; it cannot stop
the trade in raw “ white spiritand it
seldom catches the illicit distiller whose
name is legion and whose product is the
condensed essence of the bottomless pit.
» * •
The community has long endeavored, in a
feeble sort of fashion, to devise some remedy
for this state of tilings without going too far
out of i its ordinary rut, or interfering too
much with the tired old fetish of “private
enterprise.” Every time the question has
come up the importer has announced confi-
dently, in his own interest, that the high
import duty is the cause of all the trouble,
and through much reiteration of this state-
ment the public has at last come to believe
in it, just as the public believes in anything
which is told to it with sufficient confidence
and persistence. In support of this theory,
Inspector Lenthall, of Sydney, recently
pointed out that in Victoria, where the duty
on spirits is 2s. more per gallon than in N.S.
Wales, the quality is even worse, and that in
Britain, where the duty is from 2s. to 4s. less,
the quality is of the best. Unfortunately,
these statements rest on no very certain evi-
dence. It is a well-known fact that spirits
which are refused admission to Victoria as
being unfit for human consumption are often
sent on to Sydney, where there is far
less effective supervision, and therefore it is
evident that the cornstalk article is, in some
cases at least, the worse of the two. More-
over, the recipes according to which the
N.S. W. publican is instructed to make’his
bogus liquor are the same which circulate in
Victoria, and the drinks produced according
to those recipes are so utterly villainous that
it is almost impossible to conceive how any-
thing worse could be produced by human
ingenuity. Again, though the Victorian Cus-
toms tariff on imported spirits is higher than
that of Britain, its excise is decidedly lower,
therefore, according to this process of reason-
ing, Victoria must have a particularly loath-
some brand of imported drinks, and an
exceedingly excellent brand of local manu-
facture which would necessarily drive the
imported iniquity out of the market. Con-
sequently, if Victorian spirits are especially
bad, as alleged, despite the low excise duty,
it goes to prove that bad spirits are not the
result of high duties, but come to pass
whenever the opportunity offers. As for the
inspector’s childlike faith in the quality of
British alcohol, it also rests on no sure
foundation. A few years ago, Scotland, the
home of the finest whisky on earth, was
shaken by an awful series of revelations as
to the quality of the liquor sold by the
retail publican - exactly the same series of
revelations as those which now trouble New
South Wales. In the course of that up-
heaval every one of the trade iniquities of
Sydney came to light over and over again—
to say nothing of several other iniquities
which have not yet been publicly mentioned
in this country. Since then the evil has
either been mitigated by vigorous super-
vision, or else the community has ceased to
kick against it and regards it as past remedy;
at all events, the subject seems to have
dropped.
*
Even as a mere matter of figures, the
theory that a lower tariff must necessarily
lead to good liquor is distinctly illogical.
Under the present tariff a genuine spirit
may cost, duty paid, 325., while the Bel-
gian imitation costs 275., making a profit of
15| per cent, in favor of the latter. If the
duty were reduced one-half, the good spirit
would cost 20s. and the imitation 155.,
making the profit on the latter 26 per cent.
If the duty were reduced by three-quarters,
the profit on the bogus article would be 42
per cent. A lower duty might possibly tend
to reduce illicit distillation, though even that
is doubtful, for it is hardly possible to get the
duty so low that the illicit trade will not
yield a handsome profit; but it would
distinctly tend to increase the importation
of poisonous alcohol, and the only
difference would be that a local industry
would be injured to assist a foreign one
equally nefarious. Except by the creation
of a State Drink Department, there is no
visible remedy for the existing evils. When
the State makes or imports its own liquors,
analyses them, and sells them, and when it
is the only importer and wholesale and retail
dealer, then the market for German, Belgian,
or illicit alcohol, and for local “blends” of
vitriol, and the like, will be effectually
stopped. No one can import these articles,
for all importations, except those of the
State, Avill be stopped. No one can sell them
to the retailer, for the State will be the only
retailer. The remedy is, at once, simple and
effective. Its only disadvantage is that it
lies outside the old, dismal rut of “private
enterprise,” and the very idea of getting out
of an old, respected, well-established rut is
abhorrent to the public soul.
The Costa Rica Packet Case.
Most Australians have been, during the last
three years, vaguely familiar with the name of
the Costa Rica Packet. The average reader
has dimly understood that by that name there
hung a tale of wrong inflicted by the Dutch
Government upon an Australian captain in the
Malay seas; of vain Australian efforts to
obtain compensation for that wrong ; and of
the British lion crouching with his tail between
his legs before the spectacle of a gross affront
to a British seaman and the British flag. But
the documents in the case, comprised in the
New South Wales Legislative Council return of
Sept. 20, 1894, only in part justify this under-
standing. The Bulletin is not enamoured of
the British connection ; it gives full credence
to Captain Carpenter’s account of the outrage
to which he was subjected ; it recognises that
this outrage was shown by the issue to be quite
unjustifiable; yet it considers that, on the
evidence, a large amount of wild commercial
rhetoric about the case was equally unjustified,
and that much of the irritating delay in settle-
ment has arisen simply and solely because Aus-
tralia attempted to bite off the Dutch Govern-
ment considerably more than she could chew.
* • •
On Jan. 24, 1888, the Sydney-owned barque,
Costa Riba Packet, was 32 miles from Boeroe
Island, in the Moluccas. A derelict prow was
sighted. Capt. Carpenter lowered a boat, and
part of the prow’s damaged cargo of spirits—a
few cases—was brought on board the Costa
Rica Packet. Portion of this was drunk by
the crew ; portion was thrown overboard ; and
portion was (by the crew) exchanged for 751 b.
sugar at Batjan, a Netherlands-India (Dutch)
port at which the barque next touched. Capt.
Carpenter noted the finding of the prow in
the ship’s log, which was placed at the disposal
of the Batjan port authorities; he also men-
tioned the matter verbally to the authorities ;
but he did not, as seems to have been expected
by the Dutch, state the facts in writing in the
port return which he filled up, because he did
not understand the Dutch language. Capt.
Carpenter denies that there was any mark -on
the cases, and makes no mention of any on the
bottles. The Dutch say that many of the cases
were branded “Frieser”—the name of an
Amboyna merchant, who, they allege, took over
the business which Capt. Carpenter carried on
there from 1880 to 1885. Capt. Carpenter
denies that Frieser took over his business—
which, he says, he simply closed. However,
the first and second officers of the Costa Rica
Packet, while affirming that there was no
mark on the cases, say that on some of the
bottles there was a trade-mark which Capt.
Carpenter told them was his own mark when
trading, at Amboyna. At all events, the
Chinese who bought the spirits complained that
many of the bottles were filled with salt water,
and the Oontroleur of the port dismissed the
complaint as trivial. But he subsequently
wrote to Frieser, at Amboyna, telling him of
the finding of the prow and the sale of the
spirits ; and Frieser complained to the Court
of Justice at Macassar. First question : How
did the Controleur know the prow belonged to
Frieser, if the marks were insufficient for
identification ? If they were sufficient for the
Controleur, why were they not sufficient for
Capt. Carpenter? Did he not know what
firm at Amboyna was using his old trade-mark?
* * *
Second question : Supposing even that Capt.
Carpenter could not identify the owner of the
spirits, was he justified in permitting his crew
to use them and sell them ? The Dutch say
No; and quote Ferouson on “International
Law,” part 1, p. 345 :
If ships or goods have been salved or picked up at sea,
without either the master or other commander or the
owner of the cargo being present or known to the sah ois,
the objects salved shall be transported to the place neares.
to that of the salvage and delivered to the chief magis-
trate (17 and 18 Vic., chap. 104, sec. 45).
And this seems reasonable. Of course, it is the
custom of mariners to consider flotsam lawful
booty, to be used at discretion; but such a
view cannot be defended at law. Supposing
the cargo of the prow had been gold coin, would
Capt. Carpenter have been right in giving
some to the crew, throwing some overboard,
and permitting the sale of the remainder ? And
does the value of the cargo make any difference
to the principle involved ? The Dutch say
No; the action of Capt. Carpenter was an
offence against Dutch law and international
law, which the Netherlands-India tribunals
were competent to deal with.
« « •
And here, apparently, is where the Dutch
was wrong. For Netherlands-India jurisdic-
tion extends only three miles from the coast,
whereas the alleged piracy took place 32 miles
from the coast. The Dutch were misled by the
inaccurate, perhaps biassed, evidence of a pas-
senger on board the Costa Rica Packet. But,
considering the trivial nature of the offence,
and the obvious bond fides of Capt. Carpenter
regarding a cargo which could never have paid
him for salvage, his technical fault might well
have been passed over. The matter was, in
fact, allowed to lie in abeyance for nearly three
years —until Nov. 1, 1891, when Capt. Car-
penter was arrested at Teruate, though he had
been at that port twice since the alleged
piracy, in 1889 and 1890, and at Macassar in
April, 1888, three months after it happened,
without hearing anything of his alleged offence.
He was sent from Ternate to Macassar, 1000
miles away, by steamer, which left on Nov. 6
and arrived on Nov. 16. At Macassar (the
Dutch laws not admitting of bail) he was im-
prisoned till Nov. 28, when he was released —
partly, it appears, owing to the representations
of the British authorities at the Straits Settle-
ments, and partly because the Dutch found the
evidence did not warrant the contention that
the alleged offence took place in their waters.
Capt. Carpenter asserts that after leaving
Ternate he was harshly treated, classed with
the lowest criminals, and subjected to in-
dignities which injured his health. This the
Dutch deny, asserting that he was treated just
as a Dutchman would have been in similar cir-
cumstances. His child was permitted to stay
with him. Three officers, whom he took from
Ternate as witnesses, had their passages paid
by the Government; though the accommodation
provided, both for them and for Capt. Car-
penter, was third-class and very bad.
* « *
On the whole, there seems no doubt that
Capt. Carpenter was very hardly used, with
only a shadow of justification. The Dutch
claim the right of arrest on “ reasonable sus-
picion,” but the circumstances disclose no
ground of “ reasonable suspicion ” warranting
the imprisonment of Capt. Carpenter, particu-
larly when the alleged offence was committed
outside the jurisdiction of the Dutch courts.
Capt. Carpenter is certainly entitled to com-
pensation, and the owners, officers, and sailors
of the Costa Rica Packet, who were jointly
interested in the whaling cruise, should also be
compensated for the loss of time or loss of
skill when the captain’s services and those of
three officers were unavailable. But whether
the claim should reach the stiff figures of
.£lO,OOO for Capt. Carpenter, .£lO,OOO for the
owners, and £5OOO for officers and crew, may


justly he questioned. The figures are defended
on the ground that expected whaling profits
were missed, and that the Costa Rica
Packet lay so long at Ternate that she had
to be sold at a loss. But for these
things Capt. Carpenter himself seems to be
mainly responsible. Third question : Why did
not Ca'pt. Carpenter, when arrested, permit his
vessel to continue the cruise in charge of two
officers? It is said they could not find the
whales, whose whereabouts was known only to
Captain Carpenter. Could not Captain Car-
penter have communicated his knowledge?
But 'the vessel’s whaling-master says that
during her detention at Ternate he was “ fre-
quently informed by captains and officers of
steamers trading among the Dutch possessions
that they\had seen great numbers of whales a
short distance away.” (Return, p. 20.) If
these captains and officers could see the whales,
could not the Costa Rica Packet’s officers ? Or
why did not the vessel follow the captain to
Macassar, in readiness to take up the cruise
when he was acquitted, as from the flimsy
nature of the charge he must have believed he
would be ?
* * *
And, fourth question : Why did not Captain
Carpenter return immediately to his ship
when released, and continue the cruise, making
official claim for compensation in writing, re-
porting t ie fact to his owners in Sydney that
they might take action, and leaving personal
pressure till the cruise was over? Why did he
leave his ship rotting at anchor for five and
a-half months, while, without going near the
ship, he went round to Sydney via Singa-
pore, and deputationised G. (then) Republican
Dikes? The Dutch gave his three officer
wdtnesses passages back to Ternate, and
would doubtless have provided a passage for
Capt. Carpenter, also. He was released at
Macassar on Nov, 28, and says there was no
boat to Ternate till Dec. 20—a delay of 22
days, though at Ternate he had only waited
under arrest six days for the steamer to
Macassar. He says, too, that the return
voyage would have taken 16 days, against 10
days to come. Grant all this, he would still
have reached Ternate on Jan. 5. The whaling
season would have been practically lost, though
something might have been done in the three
weeks which Capt. Carpenter estimates as
remaining (Return, p. 8). But a second season
was anticipated, subsequent to January and
before June, the profits of which Capt. Car-
penter estimates at ,£13,000 in his statement
of loss (Return, p. 35). Why did he not go
ba«k to Ids ship and secure that ,£13,000? His
claim* against the Dutch would have kept till
June, and could have been pressed in his
absence. Or, if he was determined not to go
back, why did he not hand over the ship to his
five officers, with instructions where to fish in
his absence ? Supposing Capt. Carpenter had
died instead of being arrested, would the ship
have been allowed to lie at anchor till shebecame
unserviceable, or would the officers have sailed
her back to Sydney without making an attempt
to carry out the objects of the cruise?
• • «
The Dutch idea is that Capt. Carpenter
(who is by birth a Yankee from Newhaven,
Connecticut, where The Buleetin guesses folk
are pretty spry) “ viewed the affair from the
beginning as a kind of milch cow.” Anyhow,
while he was absent at Sydney, his ship, officers,
and crew were idle at Ternate, the men ailing,
the gear rotting, and the vessel spoiling. Capt.
Carpenter returned, according to his whaling-
master’s account, on April 13, 1892 (according
to his own account, on May 1) ; the Costa Rica
Pa ;ket left for Singapore on May 3, arrived on
May 18, and was sold “ at a great sacrifice,” a
few days after, for £1250. As her value nine
months previously was put at ,£3588, it is diffi-
cult to see why she was not sailed back to
Sydney and refitted, in order to make the esti-
mated profit ©f ,£23,400 next year. Probably
it was thought necessary to square accounts for
the claim against the Dutch. When that claim
reached the British Foreign Office it was cut
down from £25,000 to £2500 for Capt. Car-
penter’s personal damages, though the owners
and crew are fairly entitled to compensation
for probable loss during the time Capt. Car-
penter was compulsorily absent. The Dutch
repudiated liability, but offered to refer the
matter to arbitration ; and this offer has now
been accepted, apparently in respect of the
original claim of £25,000, although G. ex-
Republican Dibbs thought it a cumbrous and
expensive way of dealing with so small a sum.
What the alternative was he didn’t say. Per-
Ifkps, had that painter been cut, Australia
might now be engaged in a war with Holland
id the instigation of the infuriated Chambers of
Commerce. The Costa Rica Packet case doesn’t
seem quite worth that. And, by the way, sup-
posing (fie arbitrators judge the claim excessive,
who will be called upon to pay the costs ?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Monotony of Man.
A Farmers’ Association "Convention, held at
the Kanakaland township of Mackay, has en-
ihmai’acsfii.» h ; i v,
lution -- remarkable reso-
mi , * . ~ ,
mat, in view of the increasing number of employers of
labor, and the difficulty of obtaining labor, the Govern-
ment he requested to forthwith renew the assisted ini mi-
it ration of agricultural labor from Europe, or else facilitate
t ie introduction of New Guinea labor.
The audacity ot the italicised phrase is colossal,
Many hundreds of white men are tramping to
and fro along the coast districts of Queensland,
fight in among the nigger-driving, slave-renting
sugar-growers, and are ready and willing to work
at any living wage, but unable to obtain employ-
ment. The wages offered by these Farmers’ Asso-
ciation delegates range from about ss. per week
to 15s. per week in extravagant cases. And
note this: The men who thus call for cheap
and ever cheaper labor are not capitalists—not
hat Men. A few years ago they were all
laborers ; to-day, they farm patches of 50 or
100 or 200 acres, with the aid of three or four
Kanakas and such whites on the point of star-
vatiou as they can induce to accept their miser-
able terms. At the same time every w orking-man
m Kanakaland is taxed heavily to support these
renegade workers who are now eager to tyran-
nise over the class from which they have lately
sprung. The 'North Eton central mill, which
last season made a gross profit of .£lO,OOO
(depreciation and interest not deducted), and
the Race-course central mill, which made a
similar profit of £9OOO, were both erected at
the cost of Government. The money was lent
on security which would have been worthless
had not the industry proved splendidly profit-
able, and with the understanding that white
labor only was to be employed. This under-
standing was almost immediately set at nought;
and the profits which the farmers have made
have been earned almost entirely by Kanaka
labor. Now, while asking for a further supply
of cheap immigrants, they demand also that the
Kanakas be forbidden to take more than a
certain wage, and forbidden also to remain idle
longer than a definitely-limited period. They
desire, in short, an exact reproduction of the
English legislation of the Middle Ages, which
fixed in the interest of employers the conditions
of labor and the rate of wages ; and the cir-
cumstances afford another illustration of the
immutability of the human atom. The passage
of several centuries has not altered in the least
the instinct which leads man to exploit his
fellow-man. Here we have laborers who, with
the assistance of other laborers, have become em-
ployers, doing their best to degrade the men of
their own country and to limit the freedom of the
aliens on whose blood and sweat their fortune
flourishes. Yet socialists tell us that all our
troubles spring from the current competitive
system, and that to abolish human misery it is
merely necessary to abolish that system. It
never seems to strike them that this system is
merely a product of creator man, and that an
imperfect product implies an imperfect creator.
The competitive system, with all its blots and
errors, is a faithful reflex of the competitors.
Pure collectivism, if ever it could be estab-
lished, would still have to deal with a com-
munity of individuals guided by self-interest,
and the millennium would remain as distant as
ever. Only the slow process of evolution can
eradicate the monotonous meanness of mankind.
The “Purified” Elector.
1 he Purification of Rolls Act, which old James
Munro rushed through the Victorian Parlia-
ment during his brief and ignominious Premier-
ship, promises ere long to disfranchise the
whole Victorian population. The Act is a
cmious and elaborate complication. The rate-
payers roll is made up at certain regular—or
irregular—intervals, and 18 months later the
electoral roll is made up, the ratepayers’ roll
being used as a basis. No man whose name is
not on the ratepayers’ roll for the same house
which he occupies when the electoral roll is
made up, can be registered as an elector. If he
has removed during the interval, even though
he has only removed to the house next door, he
is disqualified. He may, since his removal,
have got on to a new ratepayers’ roll for his
new residence, but that doesn’t count ; he must
occupy the same premises which he held when
the old roll was made up, or he can’t vote.
Consequently, the qualification for a Victorian
elector now is somewhat as follows : He
must be a natural - born or naturalised
British subject, out of gaol, in his right mind,
and not convicted of any serious crime. He
must have lived for about two years in one
house. He must have been properly registered
on the ratepayers’ roll when he entered that
house. When the officer came round to make
up the electoral roll he must have been at home,
and able to prove his identity, and his name
must have been accurately copied from one roll
to the other. If he has been objected to by any
meddlesome person, and a notice to that effect
has been sent him, and it happens to have been
delivered—which it often isn’t, and the notice
calls on him to appear at some place and time
not clearly stated, before some court not speci-
fied, and prove that the objection, the nature
of which isn’t even mentioned, is unfounded—
that being the regular process now in Vic-
toria, and he succeeds in finding out
when and where he is to appear, and loses
one or two days’ work in attending the
court’s convenience, he must then be able to
prove that the objection—the nature of which
he doesn’t know so that he can bring no evi-
dence to confute it—is unsound. Then he
must take his chance of having his name
omitted by accident after all. And if he
succeeds in escaping all these snares and pit-
falls, and the authorised functionary doesn’t
refuse him his electoral right at the last moment
for no visible reason whatever, then he becomes
a full-blown elector of the province of Victoria.
To become a Victorian voter nowadays is about
as simple a process as catching an echo by the
tail or setting fire to a cataract.
Tariff Comedy.
A certain amount of comedy attaches even to
the serious-minded Victorian Tariff Commis-
sion. The Commission itself is a profoundly
sedate affair, but many of its witnesses belong
to the giddy order of unintentional humorists.
Hie humor lies mostly in their blank and awful
ignorance, which is, presumably, only a fair
specimen of the blank and awful ignorance of
the community. On second thoughts, however,
it is probably not a fair specimen; the illiterate
testifiers before the Board are mostly trained
experts who apparently don’t know; the simplest
matters affecting their own business, and when
an expert doesn’t know' even his own business
a person who isn’t an expert can’t be rea-
sonably expected to know anything. Among
recent witnesses, one of the most eloquent
was an importer named David Altson, who had
been ruined by that Protectionist iniquity,
the stock-tax. This duty had struck his
business such a blow that his wage-sheet
declined straight off from £BOOO to £3OOO per
annum. Therefore he wanted the stock-tax
taken off at once. When he had quite exhausted
himself it was pointed out that, according to
his own books, the collapse of his business took
place years before the stock-tax was put on,
and that his trade revived again when that
duty came along. The astonished witness then
retired in confusion. Another man came for-
ward and swore that his industry was ruined by
the wholesale importation of saddle-trees, and
unless the inrush of these articles could be
stopped, he would be utterly lost or there-
abouts. The Commission then looked up the
Customs returns, and found that last year
exactly 48 saddle-trees were imported into Vic-
toria, and 12 of these were re-exported, leaving
only 30 in the country. This witness then
apologised in great bewilderment and dispersed.
Some other witnesses—quite a stream of them,
in fact—declared that they were much injured
by the heavy duty levied on reapers and
binders, which made the prices so high as to be
almost prohibitive, and when these persons
were informed that there was no duty whatever
on reapers and binders, and that the high price
was the work of a Freetrade importing ring,
they were petrified with astonishment, and
remarked that they had been “ misled.” Also,
various people, at different times, complained
bitterly of the iniquitous duty charged on
certain dairy implements, and when they learned
that the articles in question came in free, they
also went home in a badly broken-up condition.
Considering that these are samples of the people
who come forward to shed a light on the tariff
question, it is interesting to speculate on the
possible state of the people who stay away
because they don’t feel sufficiently intelligent
to tackle that intricate subject. This opens up
such vast possibilities of illiteracy, that the
subject had better be dropped just here in
despair.
The 15-Man Cabinet.
Wm. M'Oulloch, M.L.C., is to be the new
Victorian Minister of Defence, vice the bump-
tious and belligerent Sargood. M'Culloch is
chiefly notable because it is commonly sup-
posed that he isn’t any stupider than his
colleague, Cornelius Job Ham, but otherwise
there is nothing in particular to distinguish
him from other men. In other provinces the
dullest nonentity of the Cabinet is made Post-
master-General ; in Victoria the biggest and
most unpleasant nonentity is made Minister of
Defence, and the next one is appointed P. M.G.
Victoria, by the way, is the only Australian
province which keeps a Minister of Defence,
and the only one which keeps a Minister
of Water Supply. Australian Ministries are
things of infinite variety. There are only
eight separate billets in the W.A. Cabinet.
There are 13 separate portfolios in the New
South Wales Ministry; in the Queensland
Ministry, 11; South Australian, 9; Victorian, 18.
There are sometimes as many as 15 men in a
Victorian Cabinet, while S.A. gets through
with six, Queensland with seven, and New
South Wales with ten. In addition to the
ordinary members of a Cabinet, the Vic.
Government includes, as already mentioned, a
Minister of Defence and a Minister of Water
Supply; also a Solicitor-General, a Commis-
sioner of Trades and Customs, a Minister of
Health, a President of the Board of Land and
Works, a Vice-President of the same, and a
Minister of Agriculture. Also, it sometimes
includes as many as four supernumerary Minis-
ters without salary or portfolio, who take
office for the pleasure of printing “ The
Hon. John Smith,” in big letters, on
their portmanteaux when they go travelling.
Victoria will never get properly started at the
work of retrenchment until it abolishes the
Minister of Defence and the Solicitor-General,
and ejects from the Cabinet the Minister for
Water Supply, and the Vice-President of the
Board of Land and W orks, and the Postmaster-
General, and all the supernumerary Ministers,
and gets the Cabinet down to a plain working
basis.
Mr. Saul Joshua, Melbourne distiller, simplv
dances on the Freetrade allegation that local
spirit manufacture is unduly subsidised by the
concession of 4s. per gallon in excise duty.
Victorian whisky yields ,£50,000 per annum less
in revenue tax than the same quantity of im-
ported ditto would pay. “But,” says Mr.
Joshua, “ the manufacture of beer in Victoria
for 1893 was over twelve million gallons. The
amount of money ‘ lost to the revenue ’ by not
charging this with the same duty as if it had
been imported was .£450,000 for one year.”
Apart from the absurdity of making “revenue”
a first consideration in the fearfully and wonder-
fully complicated question of liquor, there is
the Freetrade fallacy that Victoria has been
“losing money” by Joshua Bros, for years
past. As a matter of fact, the duty paid upon
native spirit to-day is Is. per gallon more than
the duty on the imported article in 1884. If it
were ss. a gallon less—what then ? A com-
munity is always prepared to expend its full
amount of available drinking money. The fact
that Victoria loses £50,000 a year by not taxing
local spirits ss. a gallon more, is merely on a
par with the equally obvious fact that the
province loses £1,400,000 a year by not trebling
the spirit-duty all round, and that it loses
£7,000,000 a year by not increasing it ten-fold.
Also, we are brought face to face with the
dreadful fact that it loses £21,000,000 a year
by not increasing it thirty-fold. The cost
of alcoholic liquor to the consumer is mainly
the amount of tax charged upon it. The
country can, and will, pay all the tax it can
afford, taking one year with another, and no
unnatural raising of the tariff, or tinkering with
differential duties, will make the blessed revenue
much richer or poorer. Whether the welfare
of the country’s stomach may be affected by
injudicious legislation is the question that no
Government ever gives a thought to.
Sydney Daily Telegraph points out that the
fact that J.P.-ships “should be the reward of
electioneering service, as they undoubtedly are,
• • • . is a satire upon the laws which
these political ‘justices’ have to administer.”
But that is a feeble satire compared to the
heavier fact that the Attorney-General, who is
also a grand jury of N.S. Wales, or Victoria, or
Queensland, as the case may be, holds his office,
from which all active “ justice ” (so-called) may
be said to flow, merely as a political reward,
and frequently because of a political neces-
sity. There is nothing to prevent, but every-
thing to encourage, Beelzebub, Q.C., and
Lucifer, Q.C., holding this legal port-
folio alternately. A satire? “Govern-
ment by Contradiction ” is a beehive of satires.
What makes an Attorney-General? His
law? No, his club. What in N.S. Wales
makes a Minister of Mines and Agriculture?
His geological and botanical attainments ? No,
his malleability and ignorance. What makes a
N.S.W. Secretary for Lands? His agrarian
culture? No, his solidity with his constituents
and his fidelity to his party. What makes
a N.S.W. Minister of Justice? His machine-
like and unerring judgment ? Not so. His
ready impressionism and Conservative idolatry
of ancient fetishes of “ the party.” Whalb
makes anything administrative? Blind, un-
questioning, reward-seeking, reward-grabbing
voting power. A satire ? The whole game is a
satire.
At Melbourne, a few days ago, an exceedingly
swell Chinaman, with many diamonds upon
him, was charged with brutally assaulting an
aged European, by driving a big door-key into
his ear and mashing him about the head with
the same weapon. He was fined .£3 and 30s.
costs. The injuries would probably have killed
a man of feeble constitution, in which case the
gaudy Chow would doubtless have been hanged;
as they didn’t prove fatal he was fined 60s,
Australian law is exceedingly particular about
murder, and hangs the murderer with remark-
able punctuality, but almost any brutality short
of murder—anything in the way of blinding a
victim, jumping on his chest and head, staving
in his ribs, ot otherwise making him a cripple
for life—can generally be compromised by a
payment of 60s. or so. That is to say, it can
be so compromised, except under two special
sets of circumstances. The policeman who runs
you in after a murderous assault may put it
down as “assault” only, in which case the
aforesaid 60s. applies ; but, on the other hand,
an especially saturnine constable may get hold
of you after a much less serious shindy and
charge you with “assault with intent to
murder,” and then you are probably rushed off
to the gallows in the shortest time on record.
Or, on the other hand, instead of horribly
mangling a man out of pure cussedness, you
may merely shake him a little and take six-
pence out of his pocket, in which case you pro-
bably get three years and 20 lashes. But if
you take the precaution of kicking the other
man so hard that he doesn’t recover his senses
for a month or two, so that he isn’t aware that
you have taken the sixpence from him, the
proceeding probably costs only 40s. The law
is a curious animal—more curious even than the
bunyip or the ornithorhynchus.
In Sydney, the other day, a lawyer turned up
in a light summer suit to defend a client charged
with a capital offence, whereupon the Judge
promptly adjourned the case for an hour, and
sent him home to put on lugubrious raiment, on
the ground that his butterfly costume was not
“in keeping with the gravity of the charge
against the prisoner,” or something like that.
When the legal person returned in hjs most
sombre “ clobber,” the case proceeded. This
kind of small interference isn’t even amusing,
and, besides, it looks like prejudging the
case. If the prisoner was an innocent
man wrongfully accused, then it was ap-
propriate that he should be represented by a
solicitor dressed in white, and the Judge
couldn’t tell at that stage whether he was
wrongfully accused or not. The average
Judge’s idea that everything connected with
justice should be of a solemn black hue is a
wicked reflection on the law’s murkiness.
Moreover, the Judge himself, The Bulletin
has reason to believe, wears white underclothing
and a pale boiled shirt, and these things are not
in keeping with his own theories. Moreover,
the same Judge goes to see pallid ghosts at
spook-materialising meetings, which is another
anomaly. Also, the Bench is incongruous in
other ways. Some day when a red-headed
Judge sits on the Bench to hear a capital case,
some barrister will object on the ground that
his hair isn’t suited to the solemnity of the
occasion. Then there will be trouble.
The Sydney Telegraph, which, in matters of
arithmetic, may fairly claim to be the stupidest
paper in Australasia, last Saturday published
in its leader columns a shriek entitled “How
Protectionist Duties are Paid ” :
A clear explanation of how Protectionist duties grow in
the process of reaching the consumer of dutiable goods
was given by Mr. Martin, M.P., in an address delivered
at Enfield last night. He took £lOO worth of goods and a
10 per cent, tax as his basis. The Government, he said,
reckoned that £lOO worth of goods increased in value by
10 per cent, on their arrival in the harbor. Duty had,
therefore, to be placed on £llO, which made the tax 11
percent. The importer who had money invested in the
duty charged interest on this, at the bank rate of, say,
7 per cent. As no merchant calculated to a fraction
of 1 per cent, the cost of an article on which
duty was paid was usually based on the maximum rather
than the minimum. Mr. Martin gave it as his opinion
that the average merchant’s advance in advalorem goods
which had paid 10 per cent, was never less than 15 per
cent., more often 17£ per cent., and sometimes 20 per
cent. The retailer having paid this extra price must get
an additional price when he sold again. This was usually
another 5 per cent., and the 10 per cent, was thus
augmented to 22J per cent, or 25 per cent, between the
Custom-house and the consumer. A large part of the 11
percent, which the State obtained was swallowed up in
salaries, &0., paid to Customs officials, so that while the
consumer paid, say, 25 per cent., the Government re-
ceived considerably less than the original 11 per cent.
The Bulletin now invites the badly-deranged
organ to do its own calculation over again.
Assuming the paper’s own figures to be correct,
which is immensely improbable, the result pans
out thus : Duty, £11; 20 per cent, on £ll, said
to be added by the merchant, £2 45.; 5 per
cent, on £l3 45., said to be added by the
retailer, 13s. 3d. Total, less than 14 per cent,
by the Telegraph’s own figures, taken on the
highest scale, instead of 25 per cent., as
alleged. The cost of collection, by the way, is
7d. in the £, the Customs being, without excep-
tion, the cheapest of all the important taxes to
collect. Why doesn’t the great Freetrade
organ hire someone who understands ordinary
compound addition to check its figures?

My DeabMoorabinda—
Messieurs B. and B. seem bent on giving us
anachronisms, Rosamond, in Regency cos-
tume, couldn’t be caught with even the finest
chaff, but in “The Sportsman,” Mrs. Briscoe,
who is presumably right up to date, is deceived
by the very flimsiest excuses that ever insulted the
mental capacity of a young married lady. Women
must be retrograding, from a playwright’s point
of view, or else Mrs. Briscoe surely would have
worn the sandals, instead of Helen Gray’s logical
daughter. The long-tailed portion of Mrs.
Brough’s wardrobe would seem to be taking a
rest lately. In “The Sportsman,” a couple of
cotton frocks were the only wear, and in “The
Open Gate” (a bit of sentiment only poetically
possible in a neighborhood where nobody kept a
goat), a plain, though artful, grey gown (grey-
liaired woihen look their best in grey—they be-
come studies in silver then) was Mrs. B’s. attire.
Grace Noble, garbed in the dusty lines of atom-
tit, wasn’t a gay spot on the landscape, which
didn’t brighten excessively even when the long-
lost Titheradge turned up in what looked like a
deeply-dyed moustache.
As for the grooming of the men in the after-
piece, Mr. Dorrington, who paims himself like
scarlet fever or aggravated frostbite, and always
reminds me of a bonnet-pin—he has such a very
small round head on such a long body—certainly
wore the prettiest clothes. But the blades look in
vain for the last word of he-fashion from the B.
and B. young men, now that Percy Lyndal has
departed. Lyndal’s raiment was always fearfully
and wonderfully constructed. He introduced
baggy evening breeches, with a silk stripe at the
outer seams, and it was he who first wore a short
dress-coat, to the rage and envy of one of the
“ walking gentlemen,” who heard vague rumors
beforehand of this wondrous garment and deter-
mined to go one better. So he gave elaborate
orders to his tailor to make him a bob-tail, and,
when the eventful evening came, out popped
Lyndal on to the stage in the bang-tail jacket so
many fat men have since adopted, regardless of
the possible back view, and out came his rival
wearing one of black velvet. Lyndal’s triumph
was complete. “Nice smoking coat, old man,”
he said, enthusiastically. And yet men laugh at
women because of their dress rivalries.
Poor Governor Duff hasn’t found Tasmania as
healthy as most people seem to do, for he has been
“ taken ” with what one Sydney daily calls “ colic,”
andj another congestion of the liver. In horsey
parlance. Gov. Duff has shown colicky symptoms
ever since he landed in Australia, and, perhaps,
now the attack has come to the surface, His Ex.
will return to his loving subjects feeling a great
deal better. The attendant doctor attributes the
attack to a draught—not one of the doctor’s own
draughts, but the kind of thing that blows through
a window and is no respector of persons. All men
are equal when they sit in a draught. Likewise,
all men are equal after a Masonic banquet, and it
was after a Masonic banquet that the laird of
Fetteresso first fell ill. He puts it down to the
fruit.

One thing the federating Premiers did in Tas-
mania was to visit Port Arthur, where the blood-
curdling stories come from, and the feature of the
picnic outing was that the feminine odds were as
five to one aboard the Oonah. Premiers Reid and
Kingston (a large man, of the flovver-in-the-button-
hole variety) are alleged to have been most atten-
tive to the ladies on the trip. Mr. Kingston wore
white, I am told, in whicli lie must have resembled
a whitewashed wall, and in which he turned so
cold later on that he finished the day in a nest of
women’s wraps, and was, therefore, the pet of the
party. Women only really begin to love a man
when he sits with their shawls on while they pre-
tend its a balmy afternoon. It seems that our
own Georgey Porgey, in a straw hat, was a spec-
tacular disappointment to the Tasmaniennes, who
thought it was a joke at first when somebody
showed them the Premier of N.S.W. Parkes
really should have been there to hear the remarks
about it. “What! that monstrous little puff-ball
of a man !” said one of ’em, quoting Meredith.
In the gardens of the model prison the distin-
guished guests caught sight of the fruit-trees, and
a rush ensued, though I don’t think I would
fancy fruit grown in that dismal soil. The
Bananaland Attorney-General was given a leg-up
a peach-tree by Premier Forrest, while ex-Premier
Patterson shook the plums off another, and Reid,
Nelson and Go. picked them up. But what an
allegorical scene it must have made? One politi-
cian shaking down fat plums, after which his
brother politician went grovelling on hands and
knees, while another man helped his political
friend to get to the top of the tree. ’Twas a
regular Tableau Yivant.
I am in receipt of two songs. One, “Love’s
Last Appeal,” by Aug. W. Juncker, the composer
of “I was Dreaming,” is well within the vocal
powers of any mezzo soprano who can hold her
breath for three bars without exploding, and the
melody has quaintly caught the true spirit of the
prettily amorous words. Flo. Esdaile sang it in
the pantomime at Her Majesty’s, where it always
met with success. The other song is a football
ditty called “Crom-a-boo” (why not “Scrum-a-
boo ”?) and deals with the plaster-of-Paris aspect
of that merry game. It is not a drawing-room
song, but anywhere in the opera with a strong
chorus “ Crom-a-boo ” might have a terrific
success.
If the Law piles much more clothing on to
itself it will become necessary for the “great”
dailies to send special frock-reporters to
the Courts when these places of public
amusement resume busings after vacation.
At the opening of N.S.W, Banco Court
last week, for instance, when women crowded
the court attired in bright array, nobody
had eyes for anybody but the Judges in their
royal red robes picked out with rabbit-skins—
which always remind me of flayed sheep with
part of the wool sticking on, and a dazzled law-
reporter drew public attention next day to the
fact that “Mr. Justice Owen wore the finest gown
of all.” But it’s an odd thing that the Adminis-
trators of the Law should make such gaudy spec-
tacles of themselves, considering that Justice
herself is always depicted witli so little on ! I
suppose they do it to keep distinct. Sometimes
when a Judge has a very square mouth and a
bullet head, I suppose the beads and wampum are
necessary to prevent Policeman X making awk-
ward mistakes.

H.R.H. Prince Joseph of Battenberg arrived per
Rome, yet nobody official seems agitated. When
your Governor is down with the stomach-ache,
your Admiral is absent, and your Mr. Mayor
isn’t quite broken to the collar, it seems to me
that a distinguished visitor stands a very fair
chance of getting a little amusem -nt out of his
trip round the kawliaies. Darling Point won’t
let the poor Prince feel bored, at any rate. When
the Duke of E linburgh was here (only, mind you,
I wasn’t born then), he was stopping the night at
a big-wig’s house, and laying abed in the morning
beheld the door swing open, and his host’s pretty,
dashing daughter tumbled into the room and then
rushed out again. She had been skylarking outside
the door with some girls and “they” pushed her,
so she said.
I hope somebody will give Prince Battenberg a
few tips as to his behavior in Australia. He
should always remember to leave bis sacred brush
full of hair when he’s spending the night any-
where, so that adormg women can make true-
lovers’ knots of his German locks, and keep them
for ever and ever until the next prince comes
along. His shaving-lather should never be care-
lessly thrown away, and any old boots, false teeth
an 1 hollow pill-boxes should always be left in con-
spicuous positions, for their price is far above
rubies. To be really popular a prince must leave
as much flotsam and jetsam behind him as if he
were a new reading of the “ B ibes in the Wood ”
and wanted to find his way back by ’em.
The very oddest things happen to people in
Australia, For instance, a Westralian lawyer
travelled up the coast a long way the other day
to look after an important case for a Japanese
client, who, on his arrival, offered him hospitality.
When the lawyer was about to retire for the night
guess his astonishment on finding a pretty little
Yum Yum curled up in his couch ! He indig-
nately fired her out, but on rising in the morning
he found another and still prettier Mdlle. Chrysan-
themum under the aforesaid couch. If this sort
of thing catches on no timid old bachelor would
dream of retiring without looking for the woman
under the bed !

In this stage of advanced woman, when my
dear sisters show every sign of a wild longing to
wear trousers, it is genuinely refreshing to read of
that Melb. law-suit when Mrs. Daih sued Mrs.
Blank for the recovery of a petticoat. It chanced
that Mrs. Dash was at some open-air festivity,
and Mrs. Blank was there too. All at once a
zephyr that was doubtless a whisper from a
lawyer, agitated Mrs. Blank’s draperies, and the
underskirt which Mrs. Dash claimed as hers came
to view. Mrs. Blank was accused of “snow-
dropping,” but a wise Judge decided that she was
not guilty and the case was dismissed. For my
part, I wish she had stolen it. Such a truly
feminine longing for the garment the Enlightened
of her sex show symptoms of doing away with,
that she must needs “nip” it off a clothes-line
would be a promising development, it seems to
me.
The cycling woman—whom even the ’bus horses
shy at as she jig-jags over the loose metal, with
her skirts hanging like a couple of chaff-bags, and
her body bent over the steering-gear as if she was
in training for a hunchback—the cycling woman
has at least encompassed us. Ladies’ cycling
clubs are established in Sydney and Melbourne—
the Sydney collection being captained by Mrs.
Maddocks, the lady who wheeled to Melbourne,
but whom the doctor sent back by steamboat;
while Mrs. Brentnall, pioneer wheelwoman of
Victoria, is to be high priestess of the Melbourne
club, which gives its tyrst run next month.
I saw one lady—she looked like a monkey on
a grindstone—wheeling for dear life on the South
Head road, the other day, and as the horse I was
driving fairly danced with horror at the spectacle
I couldn’t help wondering how the landscape will
look when it is peppered with cycle-ladies, with
yellowish or snow-white faces and hollow eyes,
straining up the hills or coining flying down them
with their Australian feet twined lovingly over
the handle in the masculine style? Now that
ladies’ clubs have started the situation is hopeless.
Women, larrikins, and dingoes all feel courageous
only when running in a p ick, and the establish-
ment of clubs will pub grit into every backward
wheelwoman who has hitherto restricted her
prowess to garden paths. Knickerbockers come
next—though why, in these hard times, a woman
should exhibit her legs for nothing when theatre-
managers pay salaries for the very same thing, is
a puzzles to
Yours affectionately, Sappho Smith.

The new double bill ab Sydney Lyceum
does not promiss to catch on quite so brilliantly
as did its predecessor, and a judicious manage*
nient announces that it will run for one week only.
Toe fault lies chiefly with the bulkier item of the
programme, the “Open Gate,” by Haddou Cham-
bers, being a sufficient y pleasant and poetic trifle.
It concerns a maiden lady who quarrelled with her
lover and sent him away, and then, repenting, left
the gate open as a mute invitation to come back.
The gate stands open as a silent appeal for 20
years before lie returns. On the night of his re-
appearance his nephew and his lost love’s niece
have quarrelled in exactly the same fashion, and
as the finale the two lovers both come through the
open gate and make it up. The gate then closes
and remains shut ever afterwards. Miss Noble,
Mrs. Brough, Titheradge and Dorrington fill the
four characters, and the gate appears personally,
and the whole thing goes off to the satisfaction of
everybody.
# • #
The “ Sportsman ” is chiefly notable
because it calls up memories of “ Pink Dominoes ”
and the “Magistrate,” without being quite so
ingenious as either of them. It belongs to that
order of comedy in which a flippant husband goes
forth on the burst, leaving behind him a lying
tale, to the effect that he has gone to see his friend
Perkins ; and his wife, hearing of his wickedness,
goes out to tell her woes to an old friend ; and
they both get into the same house. Which pain-
ful coincidence causes the premises to become full
of compromised ladies hiding behind doors, and
anguishe 1 female voices calling for help, and being
implored through the keyhole to keep quiet or
they will ruin everything, and belated gentlemen
wandering round town in cabs and borrowed hats,
and a dreadful state of mind, and not daring to
go home. The compromised female hiding in
the next room is an item which this paper vaguely
recollects having seen before, and nothing is alto-
gether novel, except four putrid fowls —produced
as evidences of the sportsman’s exploits—which
are smelt by all the characters in turn, and re-
jected with a shudder. The “Sportsman” is
< musing enough, in a certain invertebrate fashion,
b it it suffers from a lack of material, and there is
a cei tain gradual ness about it, which is a serious
disadvantage. The company seems to feel this
a good deal, and has a somewhat martyred
expression in places. Mrs, Brough does not
play her part with her wonted enthusiasm. Miss
Rom°r gives an excellent representation of a land-
lady who has seen better days, but there is very
little of the landlady—not nearly enough to give
the piece the lift it badly requires. Messrs. Ward
and Brough do nearly everything that two rather
sketchy characters permit, and Mr. Clitherow is
about the best sample of a doddering old gentle-
man with something on his mind that The Bulle-
tin has seen for a year past. Yet, despite every-
thing that is done or left undone, about the most
original feature in the “ Sportsman ” is the
fact that it will be succeeded next Saturday by
“Aunt Jack,” with Bouoicault in the cast. The
news that it is to be succeeded by anything with
Boucicault in the cast is enough to mak“ anything
gu.
** * *
Concerning the “Union Jack,” at Sydney
Royal, The Bulletin rises to acknowledge that it
last week did that play an injustice.. There is
very little about the Union Jack in it, and it
doesn’t drivel so oppressively about that vene-
rated flag as might be expected. It is an ordinary
and respectable melodrama, in which Miss Agnes
Knights is betrayed by a gaudy rake in the parson
of Albert Norman, and thereupon beseeches him
to make her an “ honest woman. ” Norman isn’t
honest himself, and the idea of making anyone
else honest strikes linn as amusing. Bikei comes
home from sea about this time and -Irikes him
also. Two exceedingly nice girls in pretty frocks
—Misses Hilda Spong and Virgie Vivienne—roam
through the drama, each with a ghoul in pursuit
of her. Miss Kuights, looking for a quiet place to
weep and think about her honor, gets into the
wrong man’s rooms and brings unjust suspicion
upon the beauteous Inman, and there are
several chaste and elegant free fights, and Baker
is court-martialled, and is vaguely understood to
be sentenced to be fatally reprimanded, and then
executed for seven years, with hard labor, and dis-
missed from the service. He escapes through a
window, and so does Miss Spong, and Norman is
joyfully disembowelled by Cosgrove, and Miss
Vivienne is imprisoned in a lonely house beside a
moaning river somewhere, and then everything
comes right with a bang in the fourth act, and
everybody embraces everybody else. Even Cos-
grove isn’t left out, for he is seen being embraced
by two policemen in the background.
* # •
The piece is lavishly mounted, and played
by an excellent company, and is altogether a
moral spectacle of considerable merit. Misses
Hilda Spong and Virgie Vivienne, as two orphan
sisters, are an exceedingly pretty pair, and the
latter’s orphan is decidedly the best thing she has
yet done to The Bulletin’s knowledge. Also, it
is quite equal to the best things which many other
actresses have done. Baker, as the person who
comes home from sea and strikes Norman, is a
fine character, and Bland Holt is eminently
pleasing. Mrs. Bland, Miss Knights and the two
villains are satisfactory, and except for a certain
highly moral but ineffective young man in the army
there is no one on whom the haggard talon of
aspersion can reasonably be laid. Even to him
the only serious objection is that he doesn’t look
very striking as himself, and probably wouldn’t
amount to much as anybody else.
** * *
The new bill at Her Majesty’s (Sydney)
consists of that dear old salt-horse drama, “Black
Eyed Susan,” and a burlesque thereon which it
better than the original. All the same, The Bul-
letin has serious doubts about the success of the
experiment. “ Susan ” belongs to the harrowing
type of drama in which a noble but impossible
sailor shivers his timbers and dashes his tarry top-
lights with dreadful earnestness. The people who
pay for this sort of thing like to be harrowed and
want to keep harrowed as long as possible, and to
the person who can take the “Susan ” variety of
drama seriously Bert Royle’s wild, weird parodj
looks like some kind of an outrage. It is vaguelj
suggestive of “ East Lynne ” finishing up with a
skirt-dance by the consumptive Isabel, or little Eva
doingacomic song after death. Still, the large class
which appreciates “Black-Eyed Susan” is so un-
accountable that no one can say for certain what
it will do under any given circumstances. The
drama is the same solid, moral drama as afore-
time, and the burlesque is a bright entertainment
which holds the touching story of Sailor Bill up to
ridicule in a joyous, irresponsible sort of fashion.
There is much elegant ballet in it, and a large
amount of music, clog-dancing, hornpipe, and mis-
cellaneous chorus, and in a general way it is as
good as an average pantomime, and better than
some recent pantomimes which didn’t come up to
the average. A new lady. Miss Adeline Wood,
who takes the part of Susan, is painfully nervous
in places, but promises well, and Geo. Rignold
makes a large and copious William—a good d u al
too large for the ’tween decks of any reasonable
man-of-war.
** * *
“ Jimmy ” M‘Mahon’s V Haunted Swing ”
got in motion on Saturday, and lias been coining
money ever since. It is a pleasant little illusion,
which isn’t calculated to excite any ill-feeling or
to shook anybody’s modesty, and after the visitor
has been swung round several times and has dis-
covered that she—the most excited visitor is
always a she—can revolve in a series of somersaults
without her hat falling off or her skirts becoming
disarranged, the motion is rather comforting than
otherwise. For a sorrowing widow, or a maiden
with a lacerated heart, the swing can be recom-
mended as a form of mild and soothing consola-
tion, and all bereaved relatives ought to try it.
** * *
The Tivoli continues to be about the best-
patronised house in Sydney, and Rickards is now
coining money with two shows at once. The
Sakers have caught on; Messrs. Irving Sayles,
Faning, Pope and other “ cullud pussons ” have
got a solid hold on the public ; and the Japanese
jugglers are a thing of mystery and a darksome
conundrum for evermore. These individuals from
the laud of the painted fan are marvellously clever
in their art, and set the recognised laws of gravi-
tation at defiance. The Bulletin, as a paper
which has always comidered gravitation an over-
rated institution, cordially approves of them
accordingly.
