A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback- the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous us© of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats,
Woodloch’s Circus has started on a tour of
the Sydney suburbs. The three last days of the
present week are to be filled at Parramatta. The
show makes a one-night stand at Burwood on
Monday, and at Entnore on Tuesday. Wednes-
day and Thursday are to be devoted to the pre-
cipitous regions of North Shore, and Balmain will
be exploited on Friday and Saturday.
** * *
Melbourne sassiety mustered in some force
at the Bijou on Saturday, otherwise the Dacres
would have suffered a disheartening first appear-
ance in Australia. A crowded house was not a
reasonable possibility. Most people objected to
going anywhere to look at anything save a long
drink, but those who had booked dress-circle seats
came along and formed the baokbone of a demon-
stration which was almost as warm as the weather.
Umler normal conditions “ A Wife’s Ordeal,” by
G. R. Sims, will seem rather sultry. It is the
story of a virtuous woman’s desperate expedient for
saving her husband from ruin* He is a weak, uncori-
vincing embezzler, but his wife kisses the top of bis
bowed head and waters the parting with her tears
when the crash comes, and determines to pull the
poor devil through at any cost. For she had seen
him —off the stage —with a pistol pointed at his
heart. Previous to this a chartered libertine, a
recognised invader of domestic peace, essayed to
add the strong-minded matron to his list of con-
quests, but failed miserably. In the hour of
trouble he happens to try again, and the lady
hints at a surrender. He looks for no explanation
of this strange inconsistency, being a languid
millionaire sentimentalist with a wide experience
of the other sex, as he supposes. Of course he
doesn’t know he is being dealt with by a devoted
wife who must raise £5OOO within a week.
• • »
Unhappy Mrs. Branscombe has one
humorous chance of getting the coveted sum by
respectable means. Her father, a broken-down
gambler and military gent, is allowed to rush
off to Monte Carlo, taking £3OO in cash, the
savings of her unmarried sister (father and sister
live with her), in order to break the bank. The
Colonel believes in a new “system” he has dis-
covered, and promises to return at once with the
proceeds. The bank breaks him. His daughters
telegraph for news of his luck, and he replies :
“Lost everything.” Then the wife assumes a
very black veil, and presents herself at the cham-
bers of Lothario saying, in effect: “ Consider
yourself my lover when you have given me a lot of
money.” He has in his desk a receipt for the
£5OOO he had left in charge of the lady’s husband
at the office of his stookbroking employers—the
financial working of the plot is rather compli-
cated —and she takes the document eagerly.
* « «
Lothario is not a beast of prey. Per-
ceiving that he has bought the lady under dis-
tressing circumstances, he refuses to claim delivery
of the goods, so to speak. The wealthy masher is
glad to pay £5OOO, or more if necessary, for the
pleasure of being magnanimous to a self-sacrificing
woman. Then the husband comes to the cham-
bers to confess to his theft, and explanations
ensue. Also, the artless younger sister comes, on
her own account, to borrow £5OOO from Lothario
and put things straight. He behaves handsomely
all round. He becomes the affianced husband of
this confiding girl, whose married sister he was
pursuing all through the piece, and he is very
severe upon one of the stockbrokers, a shrewd
Scotchman, for having expected to discover the
defalcation aforementioned. The ending is un-
natural but happy. By the way, nobody gives a
thought to the aged parent who didn’t break the
bank at Monte Carlo. He is left on his uppers in
that salubrious principality.
* • ■
“A Wife’s Ordeal ” is a clever counterfeit
of a fine play. Some of the situations are powerful
and possible, whilst the language ranges from
familiar journalese to nervous, stirring English.
Also the seducer’s cynicism is sometimes witty.
The two leading characters are thoughtfully
drawn and coloured ; the minor figures more or
less neatly sketched. The introduction to the plot
is dull and hazy, and the husband excites no sym-
pathy. Frank Cates’ feeble, mechanical perform-
ance of this commonplace person is the play’s
chief misfortune. Wilfrid Shine likewise is-un-
equal to the very short part of thegambling father,
and Miss Mabel Hardy, as a stockbroker’s amiable
lady, seems a weak spot in the cast. Elton plays
a dry, cantankerous Scotchman unobtrusively.
Miss Muriel Carr, the artless sister, is just
a winsome young lady who has the face and
voice of a first-class ingenue, and an evident
disposition to act up to them. A handsome
presence is half the battle with Arthur Dacre, in
his lady-killer capacity. His method of love-
making now, as of yore, is somewhat feminine and
stagey, but he converses on other subjects
most effectively, and is an interesting “swell.”
Miss Amy Roselle (Mrs. Dacre) made a brilliant
success. She is no longer youthful-looking. The
leanness of Frank Oates might have been a serious
drawback to a. less gifted actress of equally
generous outlines, but Miss Roselle soon
brought tears into critical eyes, and there-
after they could see no fault to find with the
impersonation. For the matter of which the wife
in this case is not supposed to be very young, and
Miss Roselle is an attractive woman with a still
shapely figure and fine carriage. If “ A Wife’s
Ordeal ” is to remain long on the Bijou bill it will
be because the genius of the leading artist keeps
it there. No English-speaking actress could play
such a deeply emotional part with more power or
naturalism.

Adelaide Royal was shut for a month after
the departure of the Holloway dramatic co., which
closed its season there on Friday, Jan. 18.
Slade Murray, however, has been running a
variety co. at the Bijou, with more or less success.
Maggie Moore opened in Adelaide, with
“Struck Oil,” on Saturday, Feb. 16. She was to
stay for a fortnight and then pass to Sydney for
her farewell performances prior to her departure
for America.
The Wild-Cat Column .
The Savings Bank of N.S. Wales has prospered
reasonably well throughout the past 12 months. During
1894 it has earned in interest over £172,000. It has paid
4 per cent, on all accounts closed during the year, and 5
per cent, to those depositors whose accounts still remain
open. The interest earned on investments averaged £4
18s. 2d. per cent., and the expenses of management are
only Bs. 7Jd. per cent. The deposits have increased by
£280,000 during the year, and the only depressing feature
is that the reserve-fund has decreased by over £7OO. On
a total reserve of £226,000 the amount is very small, still
it is a decrease. A full statement of the funds is
appended, which shows that they are well and carefully
invested.
* • * ♦
It is interesting to note that Victoria, with a
smaller population than N.S. Wales, had, on 31st Dec.,
£6,773,238 in savings bank deposits, against £6,620,092 in
the older province. Also, Victoria has nearly twice as
many depositors.

Sydney and Suburban Hydraulic Power Co.
pays a div. for the half year ended Deo. at the rate of 6
per cent, per annum. Nett revenue, £3875 Is. Bd., thus
applied-div., £2250 ; renewal of plant, £250 ; and reduc-
tion of preliminary expenses, £236 125., leaving £ll3B 9s.
Bd. to carry forward. The div. is one better than last
half, and the eo. appears to he moving forward—not so
quickly as prophesied, hut still surely ; and doubtless in
time its profits will about equal the 15 percent, of the
Australian Gaslight Co., although its ramifications can
never be so extended. As the co. gets better known
amongst city firms, its power will he more largely utilised,
and it only wants a little more activity in the building
trade to have all the machinery fully employed.
♦ • * *
The Victorian G.P.O. savings bank suspended
payment, the other day, at one of its Melbourne
suburban branches through an unfortunate blunder.
Customers presenting cheques, otherwise P.0.0.’5,
on the Vic. Government were besought to have confi-
dence and call again next day when they would be
paid in full. It seemed that the local tradesmen were
having a sort of semiofficial holiday, and the local
bank (with which the post-office in qmslion ‘fines
its cash business) had joined in the occasion without
giving warning. Thus the usual supply of gold was cut
off, and the Government institution, having no reserve-
fund to draw upon and seemingly no idea of raising the
wind in any unusual manner, confessed itself a “ broker”
for 24 hours.

Curt and current digest of the Companies’ Act :
" Inflate value of assets, pay a big div. (nominally out of
profits, actually out of capital), sell out and rest happy.”

burn Valley, the yield has totalled 30 bushels per acre.
* ♦ * *
The British papers are whooping again about
another remarkable rise in consols, which now stand at
104 J. But, in reality, (here is nothing especially remark-
able about it, and by following the same devices N.S. W. or
Victoria could, in the course of a few years, convert all her
public debt into a 3 per cent, stock, mostly held in the
province, and make it boom even at that figure. English
consols have a forced circulation and don’t rest on their
own merits. All the trust-funds held by public depart-
ments are compulsorily invested in them, which absorbs
nearly £120,000,000, and the amount is steadily increasing.
Then trustees, except when otherwise directed under the
old man’s will, are compelled to invest the trust-funds in
consols or one or two other favored securities, and this
absorbs nearly all the rest. The supply of consols is
decreasing, and the rush of compulsory investors is in-
creasing, so that Goschen’s 2| per cents, may easily go to
140 in course of time, instead of merely 104. Just let some
heaven-born financier try a similar experiment in N.S.W.
by passing a law somewhat as follows:—(1) All future
loans, oonver.-ion or otherwise, shall be issued at a maxi-
mum of 3 per cent. (2) All private trust moneys, except
when otherwise directed under the will of the testator,
shall be invested in N.S.W. stock. (3) All public trust
funds shall be similarly invested. (4) All banks doing
business in the province shall invest at least £50,000 in
N.S.W. stock and deposit it with theTreasureras security.
(5) All fire and life assurance offices shall make similar
investments, from £lO,OOO upwards, according to the
business done in the province. A heaven-born financier
of that type could, in a comparatively few years, make
the N.S.W. public debt a purely local concern, and save
£500,000 a year in interest besides.
* * * *
Brunswick (Melb.) Council has £27,000 of rates
uncollected, £15,000 being very ancient arrears. The
Council is almost bankrupt for want of this money, and
the prospects of getting it seem exceedingly slender.
Almost every municipal body in Australia is more or less
in the same predicament; the writer knows of several
municipalities around Sydney where rates have accumu-
lated for many years on plots of vacant land, the owner-
ship of which is unknown, and seem likely to keep
accumulating. Why shouldn’t a municipality be author-
ised to sell the land when, say, three years’ rates have
accumulated, if the owner can’t be found, or if, being
found, he refuses to pay ? The margin which has to be
allowed for bad debts makes the rate so much the heavier
to those who pay up regularly.

• * • «
A retired haberdasher has been giving away
some of the tricks of the trade. Asked why such odd
sums as 2s. lid were charged for goods he replied, “ Well,
you see, while the customer is waiting for her penny
change she sees something else she wants to buy. Charge
the even money and you’d find it would make a difference
of 10 or 15 per cent, in your sales.”
* * • •
To cope with the enormous and fa«t-growing
evil of illicit distillation, the Sydney authorities propose
at last to trace the liquor from the still to the publican’s
bar. In England, liquor in transit must be accompanied
by a permit, and both sender and receiver must keep a
stock account. Nowhere is the supervision as bad as in
Sydney.
* « * ♦
A Maoriland magistrate lately nonsuited a
photo.-enlargement fiend in a series' of cases where he
somiht to recover on orders given to him by the wives of
working men. “ You have no right,” said the unconven-
tional beak, “ to canvass amongst a class of people such
as those summoned and then bring them into Court.
Canvassers go round to women when their husbands are
at work and then bring them into Court to frighten them
into paying. The husbands are not responsible, and the
woman must have a separate estate before judgment can
be given against her.”
* * * *
The Bulletin has just heard of a Queensland
attorney who collected a hundred and three pounds
trust-funds for a widow and gave her a cheque for the
amount postdated seven weeks. Cheque duly dis-
honored. Gent One put on a friendly gent (say Gent
Two) to induce the widow to sue him (Gent One) by writ
for the amount of dishonored cheque only. The verdict
was against the widow, on the pre-arranged defence that
the Stamp Act rendered a cheque post dated a month a
promissory note, and that, as the cheque only bore a
penny stamp, the document was Valueless.
If Australia be really desirous of encouraging
the consumption of its own wine by its own people, for
the benefit of the national health and pocket, why not
establish a retail wine-trade in each province on the
Gothenburg system? No vested interests worth men-
tioning stand in the way.

A weak point in the campaign to municipalise
the Manly pier is the fact that certain aldermen are also
shareholders in the to-operative Ferry Co. The argu-
ment that their pecuniary interests are small does not
count, for the fact that, they are open partis ns is admit-
ted, and human nature will do as much for its amour-
propre os for its pocket. Advocates for the municipalisa-
tion of public works should be absolutely free from the
suspicion of gaining otherwise than as personally dis-
interested members of the municipality.
Manly pier should be banded over to neither of
the contending factions. It should he lengthened and
made into a public whatf, with traffic regulations pre-
scribed by the Marine Board, if some reasonable motive
Vivendi cannot he <stahlished between the contending
companies. Manly is a Little Pedlington, where people
are spoiling for a fight.

* * " * *
Some Sydneyites will remember with pain the
Maritoto (M.L.) mines, and will receive with grim joy the
statement that these are turning out well, and that th> y
are to he floated in England. All mines are looking well
when they reach that stage, but to tell a Svdney ex-
shareholder that a Maritoto mine will ever be rich is labor
in vain.
• * * *
Gympie mines are attracting much attention in
Sydney. Poor Gympie has always had to be satisfii d with
small things. Slowly, but surely, it has proved itself the
safest field in Australia for investment. Indiscriminate
investments would even return bank-rate of interest at
least. But Gympie has always heen cursed with some-
thing, and now that the Sunday paper’smining tipster has
started to boom its stock there is no hope for it.
• • * *
“Great activity is still being displayed at Mt.
Drysdale, which is looking as well as ever.” Then why
have the yields ceased? It is weeks since they had a
clean-up or a div. It is also stated that a lot of the
shareholders have signed a contract to sell out at £7OO
a share—a bad sign. Also, a new manager has been ap-
pointed. And, above all, the newspapers are trying
to make the public think there’s nothing wrong with
the mine which has stopped paying divs.
* • * *
Bywong, the latest N.S.W. quartz-gold rush
was last week “ feverishly anticipating ” Father Curran’s
visit. His reverence now says there are several distinct
reefs, all small ; believes they will become poorer at 50ft.,
and thinks there are already more men on the field than
present resnlls justify.
* * * •
The O’Sullivan, writing on Bywong, quotes the
Rev. W. B. Clarke as having, 40 years ago, foretold the
district’s greatness. Posterity is making the poor parson
the champion gold-boomer of N.S.W. Is there a remote
corner anywhere of which he did not predict big aurifer-
ous things? The 0 Sullivan says nothing re the size of
the reefs.
• • • *
The local warden has refused further labor
suspensions requested by the owners of the Fiery Cross,
Barmedman, N.S.W., because they have already had five
years. Now the right time has arrived for newspapers to
say: “This mine would pay handsome divs. if only it
had a working capital of £5OOO ; years ago it turned out
6000 oz. gold but work was suspended at the water-level.”
In conjunction with one or two other mines it was almost
placed in London some years ago for a heap of cash.
• • * •
There is little faith in any Hillgrove mine out-
side of Baker’s Creek. Last week they struckA rich vein
in the West Sunlight, and if, as believed, it can be inter-
sected in several levels immediately, it means a div. life
to the mine, the shares of which, notwithstanding, are
selling at 2s.
« • * •
The New Bushman’s Co., Parkes, N.S.W., is
preparing to take over the mine from the tributors next
month, and is calling tenders for a 20-head battery.
That will mean an output of 1000 tons a month—quite as
much as can at present possibly be maintained under the
most favorable circumstances.
* • • •
A start is to be made with the Rev. Campbell’s
N.S.W- co-operative gold scheme. Proposed to have 1000
£3 shares, payable ss. a month. If, as Mr. Campbell says,
men can earn right away 15s. to £5 a week, and it is only
proposed to spend £2OO in tools, why is it necessary to
have £3000? Also suggested that Govt, should erect a
battery in some central site. Where is there such a
site? Govt, aid in prospecting has been proved largely a
fraud. If Govt, will erect a State Metal Works and run a
School of Mines that is enough.

Alan Bright’s circular remarks that the recent
cutting of the rich two fort, reef at 1090 feet in
the New Bonnie Dundee shaft. Charters Towers,
has quite revolutionised values on the surround-
ing properties. Six months ago Bonnie Danders
were neglect'd at 2a.; now there are local buyers
at seven times that price. The new reef is believed
identical with that worked in the Victory. The Brilliant
reef, for which the shaft was sunk, s'ill lies underneath.
The money to sink the shaft wag raised by a special effort
by the London owni rs of the mine, and as a reward their
shares have gone from 2s. to 145., and the main asset of
their property, the Brilliant reef, still remains to be ex-
plored.

The New Victoria (Coolgardie) report says their
tailings assay over 2oz. If that represents the bulk, they
should he ashamed of themselves for losing 50 percent.
The gold, though fine, is free, and 85 per cent, should bs
saved by careful amalgamation.

Again believed the whirlwind of a Coolgardie
boom is gathering in London. Alleged mines are cheap,
alleged experts are elastic, and, above all, the public is
optimistic and the world hungers for gold.
* » * *
The Great Coolgardie is one of the London
floats with a ponderous capital. That it is also a great
mine is shown by the cabled reports appearing in Lon-
don newspapers. “ Struck rich body of ore in Lindsay’s
adjacent block south, causing great excitement; the lode
can l>e traced into the Great Coolgardie.” Now', where is
the Great Coolgardie mine in relation to Lindsay’s? and,
also, what has become of the great find, and the great
excitement caused thereby ?
* * * •
The last transformation of the Westralian
“mountain of gold ” find sounds sensible and real. The
finders, arrested for alleged nigger-shootjng, say there is a
big hill of solid quartz which will average soz. to the ton,
and enough stone expos'd to keep a 20-head battery
going three years. The public are fooled every month by
Coolgardie newspaper correspondents w»ho have lively
imaginations or drink too much whisky. Having been
left behind in the Londonderry discovery, they wera
warned not to let it occur again ; hence it is well known
by prospectors that they can he fooled into wiring across
any sort of a yarn. Another Londonderry would not be
believed now until demonstrated by bank-receipts.

who has been to the Dargo High Plains, Gippslnnd, where
there was a rush to open up what was believed to he an

A Melb. importer was passing some goods. The
entries were marked, “4 tanks, so-and-so,” and duty was
demanded on the tanks. He withdrew the entries, and
made out a fresh set—“ 4 packages, so-and-so.” Then the
tanks came in free.
• • * •
Mr. Moreton Frewen has erected a Crawford
crushing mill and amalgamator at the works of the
Austral Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne.
The feature of this plant is the exceedingly fine pulverisa-
tion of the materials and their gradual amalgamation.
The process is particularly adapted for ores carrying gold
in a fine state of division, or for what is popularly known
as‘‘float ” gold. It is also anticipated that the process
will find a very successful sphere of work in the treat-
ment of pyritic ores by mechanical amalgamation. Ex-
haustive tests are now being made on a variety of
refractory ores.

Mrs. Hannah Lloyd, Waterworks-square, Solo-
mon Town, Port Pirie, S.A., who writes on June
Bth, 1893 For about seven years I suffered with
heart disease and liver complaint. I was so ill
that I had to go to the Jamestown Hospital.
I was treated in this institution for a considerable
time, but I gained no benefit whatever; I then
consulted various doctors, but I got no better, in
fact,' I was looked upon as incurable. About four
years since, I commenced to use Clements Tonic,
with the result that I soon found I was much
better, and after continuing its use for four weeks
I believed myself quite cured. I sometimes since
have had slight attacks of pain and palpitation
in the region of the heart, when I immediately
take a dose of Clements Tonic, and am at once
relieved from suffering. In conclusion lam most
happy to state that I am in far better,health now
than ever I was since childhood. You are at
liberty to use this statement as you will. —Yours
gratefully, Hannah Lloyd, Waterworks-square,
Solomon Town, Port Pine, S.A.
MEDICAL ADVICE TO JOCKEYS.
A former crack jockey, who has grown so stout
that he had to give up riding, met the winner of
the Melbourne Cup. “ Jim,” he asked, “ how do
you manage to keep so thin? ” “I used to be as
fat as you are now,” was Jim’s answer, “but I
consulted Dr. Macnoodle, did not eat anything
rich, drank no beer, smoked only Frossard’s
Oavour cigars, and you see the result.” “ Well,”
said poor fat Charley, “I was once,as thin as’a
lamp-post. I went to Dr. Macnoodle, dlcJ not eat
anything rich, drank no beer, smoked only
Frossard’s Cavpur cigars, and—l weigh 15st.”
They discovered that what was right for one was
wrong for the othen, but they both agreedMhat a
mild, fragrant Cavonr cigar at eight Ur one shil-
ling is the best smoke one can get for the money.

The Late JUDGE TRENCH, of Victoria.
Bishop Green, of Grafton and Armidale,
N.S.W., has at last succeeded in getting the
financial affairs of his See into intelligible order,
and discovers that his episcopal income will just
reach .£175 per annum. “Nevertheless,” he
declares, “ I’ll stick to my post.”
One Williams, aged 85, has lately done a
short “stretch ” in Brisbane. He was tran-
sported in 1824 for murder when 14 years
of age. Williams was one of the occupants of the
hulk Success at Williamstown and claims to have
given the signal for the murder of Price.
The memory of the heroic Annie M‘Quaid,
the Wairarapa stewardess, has been perpetuated
by the presentation to her aged parents of a silver
life-belt, bearing the words—“ Honor the Brave.”
The old people declined to accept any monetary
testimonial.
Arthur Bayloy, the discoverer of Bayley’s
Reward, who lately bought a nice fattening estate
at Avenel, Vic., finding that he couldn’t sell his
fat stock to the local butchers save at absurd
prices, has bought the butchers out, and is now
opening shops all over the place fur the sale of
cheap meat.
Neither Premier Kingston nor ex-Premier
and Agent-General Playford has been offered a
title, because each has publicly boasted that he
would refuse the “distinction ” if it were tendered.
The Colonial Office is in such a delicate position
in these matters that it cannot afford to be
snubbed.
Evangelist Yarley: “For every godly
magistrate we have, there are 10 ungodly ones.
For every godly statesman, there are 20 ungodly
ones.” Probably so; but Varley stopped a bit
short. For every godly evangelist and preacher
there are 100 humbugs. This sort of accusation
is easy and cheap.
The newspapers have mentioned that John
Kendall, who died at Kendall Yale, Milton
(N.S.W.), the other day, was the son of a pioneer
missionary, but have omitted to state the far more
interesting circumstance that he was an uncle of
the late Henry Kendall (full name, Henry
Clarence Kendall), the Australian poet.
Apropos the recent death of Mrs. Booth,
widow of Rev. Caleb Booth, in Melb., Rev. C. P.
Thomas, preaching the funeral sermon, said she
was the youngest of a miraculous family. Her
father did not marry till he was 70, and then
wedded a lady of “ advanced age.” They had six
children, of whom five attained maturity, four
boys and a girl, all stalwart, and all over six feet.
NJ3. Wales boasts a family in whose veins
runs thickly Guelphic blood, and who bear an
absolutely startling likeness to the British Royal
family. And in N.S. Wales, also, is a great-
great-grand-daughter of Napoleon Bonaparte, a
charming and handsome girl with a straight nose,
large eyes, and Napoleonic chin and frown, a
feminine reincarnation on a stately scale of the
Little Corporal.
In Palmerston, N.T., lives an aboriginal,
“Old Solomon,” totally blind' through smallpox.
He has never seen a white man, but can name
everyone in Palmerston on hearing him speak,
and also a great many of the bushmen, whom he
only occasionally meets. He can find his way
anywhere about the town unaided, and knows the
name of every English coin, copper, silver, or
gold, by feeling it.
The Argus gives Louis Becke’s “By Reef
and Palm” a most complimentary notice, prefer-
ring Becke “in this sphere ” to R. L. Stevenson.
“ ‘By Reef and Palm,’” says the Argus, “ hardly
needed the complimentary introduction by the
Earl of Pembroke to recommend it to Australian
readers,” unconscious, of course, that the stories
were already well-known to Australian readers—
much better known to them than is the Earl of
Pembroke.
It is related of a still-sitting Australian
Supreme Court Judge that he once bought a cow,
which he sent back to the vendor on the ground
that she was too old, having no teeth in her lower
jaw. It was the same Judge who, having heard
evidence all day in a pastoral case wherein
“ brumbies ” were frequently mentioned, said to
the jury; “ But, gentlemen, who is this Mr.
Brumby ? We have heard a great deal about him,
but-have before us absolutely no evidence whatso-
ever as to his identity.”

Demise : Ex-Judge Trench, Ballarat, age
83. A frail and feeble wisp he was, a lengthy
reed, shaken by the wind, while Aspinall, Ireland,
Dawson, Fellows, Stephen, Webb, Bunny, Bin-
don, Atkins, and the rest, were in full bloom, but
he would diet himself on a strawberry a day, if
needful. Such men take birdlike sips of very dry
champagne, or old sherry, but won’t make granu-
lated Jenolan Caves of their stomachs. The
lordly Trench was a quaint spectacle as a demo-
crat, lending Berry a hand in Cabinet-making,
but, like Hori Grey, he had sound democratic ideas.
So impressed was he with the wrong-doing of judi-
cial sentences, that he published a little book on
the subject, and helped to launch the present sim-
mering for sentences to be decided by the juries,
as in America. Mining law was Trench’s forte,
and Ballarat his locale. He had a pull at all those
famous eases of the Hand and Band, Band of
Hope, Kohinoor, Sir William Don, Inkerman,
and what not, which afforded so much pabulum
for Judge Molesworth, with his sniffings, and
spider-crawls of the ink, out of the bottle, in his
familiar hieroglyphic judgments, on small, square
blue foolscap sheets tied with blue ribbon.
Thomas Joseph Byrnes, Attorney-General
of Queensland, who, at the Federal Council, moved
that Queen Victoria be prayed to promote an
Australian Judge to the Privy Council, was born
at Brisbane in Nov., 1860, and is, therefore, not
yet 35 years of age. His early political success
shows what the conjunction of luok, pluck and
ability may do for a man—ability being, in Byrnes’
case, the least of the three factors of fortune, for
his talent is of the plodding; nob the brilliant
order. He started his career on a low rung of the
social ladder, and the Q. Labor press, with bad
logic and less taste, still jibes him because as a
boy he used to carry round milk to the family
customers at Bowen. His father died when he
was still young, and Thomas Joseph owes the
foundation of his fame to his recently-deceased
mother, who spared no sacrifice to give her boy a
good start in life. He was educated at Bowen
State school and the Brisbane Grammar School,
and—always working persistently—took a State
T.P. JEN KINS
[?]
"NEW RAPIO" S[?]ty
scholarship to Melb. University, where he got his
degree and was called to the Bar in ’B4. Re-
turning to Brisbane he studied for a year with
Pat Rea], and then commenced practice under
the patronage of Griffith. He did well from
the beginning; and, later, took up the Catholic
connection which Pat Real dropped on his eleva-
tion to the Bench. His religion has always been
Byrnes’ best friend; and it was as a representative
of the Catholics that Griffith took him into his
infamous Coalition Cabinet in ’9O, as Solicitor-
General. In that year lay for Thomas Joseph
Byrnes the parting of the political ways. He is a
Democrat by instinct and conviction ; and he had
to choose whether he would henceforth nurse his
democratic opinions in obscurity, or whether he
would bow the knee to Baal as so many better
men have done. He preferred Fat Man’s cash to
the long-deferred credit of Democracy, and filled
his purse while he killed his political reputation.
He had the distinction of drawing the largest fees
paid to counsel in the notorious Robb arbitration
case; after, presumably,, as Solicitor-General, agree-
ing on behalf of the Crown to the amount to be
marked on his own brief as barrister. When
Sam Griffith became Chief Justice, after
consenting as Premier to the passage of an
Act which increased the Chief Justice’s salary
by .£lOOO a year, Thomas Joseph Byrnes was
made Attorney-General. At the ’93 election he
exchanged his Legislative Council seat for a seat
in the Assemb’ as member for Cairns, a Northern

constituency under the thumb of W. H. Swallow,
of Hambledon sugar plantation, who pulls busi-
ness and Masonic strings and generally runs the
township. Byrnes wanted a seat; Swallow
wanted a man who was safe on the Separation and
black-labor questions. Byrnes readily agreed to
what was required of him, and the thing was
done. The Labor-party put forward a man of no
particular account, and Byrnes had an easy
victory. Since then Thomas Joseph has enjoyed
a very good time; and, as it is now too late for
recanting, The Bulletin expects him, primed by
the craftier Griffith, to develop into a very con-
siderable obstacle to Australian National progress.
An elaborate manner of making a bow
To all distinguished visitors, Punch says was how
Fitz gained undue prominence. Let us allow
That Punch is correct. What a giddy old cow
Must the public have been to have thought that enow
For conceding such prominence! Bow 1 wow 1 wow.
And this is most exquisite :
And now, Mr. Punch, is it worthy of you
To assail one who’s striven to be honest and true,
And join in the howl of an envious few
Who know nothing at all of what he has to do 1
You might surely do better.—And so. Sir, adieu.
E. G. FITZ GLBBON.
T. P. Jenkins.
Though disguised in “Rational Dress,” Mr.
Jenkins’ physique is eloquent of great stamina.
He began cycling 19 years ago, started raoing 13
years ago, and has proved himself one of the
finest champions that Australia has produced.
He has won over 60 races. He beat T- W. Busst,
the English champion rniler, for the one-mile
championship of N.S.W.
In ’B7 he won the three-
mile interprovincial race in
so brilliant a fashion that
the N.S.W. Cyclist Union
and the Vic. Cyclist Union
combined in presenting
him with a handsome ad-
dress, expressing their
admiration. In the same
year he took altogether 16
prizes, including all the
N.S.W. championships.
Although now 35 years
old, Mr. Jenkins hopes yet
to put up a sensational
record. In a few weeks’
time he is going to lida
from Sydney to Melbourne,
578 miles, and will try to
eclipse Armstrong’s time
of 4 days 3| hours. It is
only a wonderful stayer
like Jenkins who could
attempt this herculean
task, for Armstrong was a
phenomenal man, who,
during his ride, rested only
six hours out of the 24,
and slept with the greatest
contentment under a wire
fence. Still, Mr. Jenkins
has many precedents of his
own from which to form a
judgment. Racing, on the
track, he was best at long
distances, but successful as
he was at 10-miles, his
road-riding for 100 miles
and upwards is still more
extraordinary. He holds
the road record for 100
miles; he has ridden 134
miles (the last 50 over a
very heavy road) in 9hrs.
22min. ; the other day he
started at 5 o’clock in the
morning and rode 117 miles in 7\ hours, after
covering which distance he felt as fresh as ever.
When he first began cycling, the old high-wheelers
were in vogue, and Mr. Jenkins rode a 60-inch
one. Now he uses the familiar low-wheel type;
the machine he will ride on from Sydney to Mel-
bourne he obtained at the Bicycle Depot of his old
opponent, 0. W. Bennett, of 159 Clarence-street,
Sydney.
The lately-deceased squatter M‘G>aw, ®f
Kooba, N.S.W., hailed from Ballymena, in the
North of Ireland, and the swagrnan who could
talk about that town was sure of a job, or of
plenty tucker if the station was full-handed. This
becoming known, the Munsterman who had never
been North of Drogheda, would say he had
“Wance passed through Ballymena.” One day,
a Chinese cook, seeking employment, exclaimed,
when all else had failed, “ All li, Missee Magaw,
me from Ballymella allee same.”
William Greenlaw, for years manager of
the Colonial Bank, Melb, and for a generation one
of the chiefs of Southern finance,"died last week.
When he got into the box in court to give evi-
dence for the bank he used to blunder terribly.
“Gentlemen,” said Justice Hartley Williams in
charging the jury in a Cblonial Bank case, “ when I
was at the bar and appeared for the bank I shivered
in my shoes whenever Mr. Greenlaw entered
the box.” Greenlaw came into power on the
deposition of Adam Burnes, the first manager,
who fell to the ground when Glass broke. Hugh
Glass was a squatter, with the bank in his toils.
Confidence in him led also to O’Shanassy being
deprived of the chairmanship in favor of Alder-
man Teddy Cohen.

At Melb. Revision Court recently, a
foreigner who, for 40 years past, had regularly
recorded his vote in Victoria, and two others who
had voted for 15 years, were struck off the rolls,
not having been naturalised.
Victoria’s latest railway—Wycheproof to
Sea Lake—has been built in the usual substantial
fashion for £l5OO a mile. The average for the
rest of the Vic. railway system was £12,665 per
juile—most of it paid away to landholders 1
The Federal Council at Hobart was asked
to do what the American Union never dared to
attempt: to adopt a resolution in favor of forcing
Prohibition on the several provinces. Some people
would make short work of State rights in the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth.

matter'we are leading the world. What are they
doing in Australia ? They are copying from M.L.
What are they doing in Europe? They are copy-
ing from M.L. What are they doing in America?
They are copying from M.L. (A voice: Lick
creation, eh?) Yes! Wo can lick creation.

Griffith’s solicitors’ reciprocity
agreement has been accepted by the
Judges of Queensland and N.S.W.,
but as the amalgamation of solicitors
and barristers' is the law in Queens-
land and not in N.S.W., interesting
difficulties are likely to arise.
Since the Tasmanian Main Line
became a State instead of a private
railway, working expenses have been
reduced 25 per Cent. Yet the old
[i e _ that State management is
necessarily more costly than private
management —looks as heal thy as ever.
One cheap method of retrench-
ment could safely be adopted by the
Yic. Govt. —viz., doing its business
correctly. Crown law is always
blundering; the Education Depart-
ment has been wrongfully dismissing
its teachers (at a cost of £3000);
Railway Dept, has just been ordered
to pay damages for wrongfully dis-
missing a stationmaster; Lands
Dept, has allowed rents to get in
arrear, £750,000, and frequently duns
the man who has paid up.
Brodribb, ex-Secretary of Yic.
Education Department (a lavishly-
pensioned officer), has just been ap-
pearing as a barrister for ex-teacher
Mrs. Mattingley, in her case v . the
Department, whereupon Minister
Peacock is appropriately sarcastic.
Look at all the bearings. Mr. Brod-
ribb uses his departmental know-
ledge, to the injury of the poor old
cow who furnishes him with her milk.
Then, again, this heavy-metalled
pensioner comes and “ snavels ” cases
out of the mouths of struggling bar-
risters, who unavoidably subscribe to
his pension.
Mr. Northcroft, P.M. at Blen-
heim (M.L.), in the course of a recent
case affecting a Government con-
tract -. “At one time I was in a
Government office where it was part
of my duty to open tenders. I was
surprised at the great difference in
the amounts of the tenders, and
asked the engineer for an explana-
tion. 1 Well,’he said, ‘ some of them
intend to pay for the work and the
material; some intend to pay for the
work only but not for the material;
Mid some don’t intend to pay either
for work or material, and these
beggars are always the lowest ten-
lerers.’ ”

Said that the once richest man on Bendigo
now runs a modest boarding-house in Melb.
Since the day he banqueted a great potentate in
his mine, and spent hundreds in decorating the
level, and specially laying gas all over the work-
ings, his fortune gradually dwindled.
.Much amusement and some indignation
has been caused in Adelaide by the perusal of
Corny Proud’s Review op Reviews article re the
S. A. Women’s Suffrage Act. His calm arrogation
of the chief credit attached to its passage is so
utterly unfounded that the ludicrous aspect of the
situation strikes most people “ in the know.”
S.A. Register says sacrifices must i>«,
made all round for Federation, and whoops for
the new Federation Leagues. Then, because it is
an Opposition paper, it stirs up animosity amongst
the farmers against Kingston’s reciprocity treaty
with Maoriland because barley is admitted free to
S.A., and the farmers get no corresponding con-
cession.
The curse coming home. Of yore, the
Briton loved India as an outlet for his manufac-
tures. The Hindoo will yet view Britain in the
same light. Indian cottons undersell Manchester.
Indian brewers supply all the beer to the Indian
army, and now India is entering the arena as a
grower of cane and refiner of sugar. Let Queens-
land beware.
It is complained against the S.A. Commis-
sioner of Police that he is afraid of asserting him-
self against either political complaints or political
patronage. Hence the disorganisation which at
times makes itself so manifest in the force sup-
posed to be under his control. He is even more
“attentive” to “distinguished” globe-trotters
than Sammy Way.
The Gothenburg licensing system, which
G. D. Clark calls an invention of the Devil, is
admitted on all hands to have diverted the Scan-
dinavian drink habit from ardent spirits to beer.
Australian advocates of that system merely desire
an analogous diversion in Australia”from ardent
spirits to wine, and, if Australian human nature
be like Scandinavian, who, as a patriot, can oppose
the experiment ?
The Royal assent was given to the S.A.
Adult Suffrage Act on Feb. 7, and the official
document intimating the consent is expected in
Adelaide about the middle of March. Then
the Sheriff will proceed to enrol the names of
70,000 women. To enable them to vote at the
general election in April the rolls must be com-
plete before the end of Sept., as names must be
registered six months.
Total population of Bundaberg, Q., dis-
trict, 13,712. Kanaka slaves (a generous esti-
mate), 2700. Total deaths, 307. Kanakas, 170;
white, 131 1 The white death-rate (including
infants) is thus 11 and a decimal per 1000, the
Kanaka, 62 and a decimal (exclusive of infants 1 11).
Yet the Australian Star, of Sydney, which
whoops for Queensland nigger-slavery, says it
wants “definite charges” made against the slave-
traffic.

The all-absorbing British capitalist, it
seems, is desirous of acquiring exclusive territorial
rights in New Guinea, and has approached Gov.
MacGregor upon thesubject. The Governor is, how-
ever, opposed to such rights being granted, as he
believes that it would be distasteful to the Austra-
lians. As a business speculation let the con-
trollers of New Guinea let the land on lease if it
is to be parted with at all. The (English) Hamp-
ton Plains Co., which lately sharked so much
valuable Westralian land, is, it will be noticed,
letting same on lease—not selling it.
Apropos ex-Premier Parkes’s fervid apos-
trophe concerning the glories of what the Aus-
tralian Star calls “the foul old rum hospital,
soaked through and through with the odours of
the bygone convict time” —which building is
identical with the Sydney (N.S.W.) Parliament
Houses of to-day—nobody has yet remarked on
the eagerness with which Parkes aforesaid, who
now so enthuses over “ the only historic building
in Australia,’’seized the chance, in centenary year,
of laying the foundation-stone of the new N.S.W.
Parliament Houses—still an institution of the dim
and misty future.
Dunedin Congregational Union has decided
against advocating Bible-lessons in State schools,
So long as the Bible is kept out of State-schools
there is some chance of the two big rival religions
remaining at comparative peace. To Biblicise the
schools is simply to Protestantise them.
Sir Henry Wrenfordsley, the “journeyman
Judge,” writes to London Times, proposing that
Australia should make its Federal Parliament by
the simple process of lumping together the six
Legislative Councils and giving them supreme
power to run the show. This great suggestion
deserves to be remembered as the most awful hunk
of imbecility yet contributed to the Federal discus-
ston. Plainly, it doesn’t require much brains to
be some kind of a Judge.

Premier Nelson (Q.) tells a Brisbane news-
paper man that, at the recent Hobart Conference
of Premiers, “ a general desire was expressed that
the Chief Justice of each province should be
eligible for election to the Convention.” Let
public sentiment thunder back “No!” It is a
case of bad varicose veins that an Attorney-
General is primarily a political bounder ; it would
be fatal blood-poisoning of the Constitution if a
Chief Justice, or any other Judge, were per-
mitted to be also a party politician. No ; Judges
must be the eunuchs of politics.
“ The Kanaka must take a smaller wage
or be replaced by the laborer who will.”—Brisbane
Courier. When will some proprietor begin to
introduce the educated and brilliant Hindoo uni-
versity graduate to be a cheap editor in Australia
and show the Courier man that he must also take a
lower wage or be replaced by the nigger who will ?
Then what a change there will be in the morning 1
It is always the man that the cheap nigger doesn’t
compete with who thinks that the cheap nigger
should compete his hardest with some other man,
and who thinks the other man a degraded ruffian
for not seeing it in the same light.
Mr. “Jack” Want, who was appointed
N.S.W. Attorney-General during the vacation,
and who straightway started for Honolulu on full
salary of <£4o a week, has gone on to ’Frisco. By
the time he returns to Sydney, Mr. Want—a
most uncompromising opponent of payment of
members—will be legally entitled to draw £6OO
without necessarily doing a single scrap of work.
As a motion of censure is very likely to be
tabled against the Reid Government soon after
Parliament meets, it is possible that the erratic
Mr. Want may return too late to do one single
stroke of the work attaching to his office—which
would be a service in this way, that no Govern-
ment would dare to perpetrate a job of similar
flagrancy for a week or two at all events.
The Sunday following the adoption of
standard time, Rev. J. Gilbertson, Anglican par-
son at Wedderburn (Vic.), (formerly of Rockhamp-
ton, Q.), thus prefaced his sermon :
My brethren,— During: the past week an extraordinary
attempt has been made by Parliament to alter the time of
day. I wish to announce that I intend to take no notice
myself of such alteration—nor will the sun do so either.
Ttie sun will rise and set as heretofore, regardless of the
unauthorised mandate of men in temporary authority. I
do not know of sufficient cause for such alteration, and I
repeat that I shall adhere to the only true time, and let
those who are weak enough to adopt this false Parliamen-
tary time do so if they wish. The text for this evening’s
sermon will be found in the nineteenth verse of the one
hundred and fourth psalm—“ The sun knoweth his going
down.”
His fine old Conservative reverence continues to
ring his bell by the old time, 20min, after the
other churches. Being a parson, he is naturally
behind the times.
The P. and O. Co.’s local manager declares
that Australia has no right to demand the aboli-
tion of Lascar labor on board the State-subsidised
P. and O. mail-boats, because the Lascars are
“just as much British subjects as are the Aus-
tralians,” and are entitled to exactly the same
privileges. Let them have the same privileges,
then. As soon as the British authorities recognise
the Indian’s equality by giving him a Parliament
based on manhood suffrage, and a nigger Ministry
to run the whole business of Government, and
entirely withdraws the British armed forces, and
as soon as the P. and O. Co. recognises the
Lascar’s equality by filling their boats with cheap
nigger captains and officers, then The Bulletin
will recognise that the Lascar is the same sort of
British subject as the Englishman and the Aus-
tralian. But for the present it states, on the
authority of the British Government and the
P. and O. Co., that he isn’t, and acts accordingly.

It is doubtful whether woman can be
elected to S. A. Legislature under the Adult
Suffrage Act. There is no section which provides
for such a privilege, but the Act provides that
women shall “possess and exercise” the same
“ rights ” as men, and it is alleged this only covers
voting. In the original bill a clause was included
enacting that women should not sit in Parliament,
and it was rejected in the Council.

In Victoria, a newly-admitted barrister
has to pay 40 guineas as a library fee, which is one
of the many methods of keeping the profession
select. It was lately proposed to reduce the fee
to 10 guineas, but the Tories of the Council threw
out the proposal with scorn and horror. Yet the
same Tories, during the maritime strike, stood
aghast when they heard that the wharf laborers
charged £5 for admission to their union to keep it
select, and called it “unexampled tyranny.”
Parkes, having become desperate, has be-
taken himself to Kiama and Jamberoo, where,
talking Federation, he quotes Coghlan to the
effect that 10 years hence the population of Aus-
tralia and Maoriland will he between six and
seven millions. By then N.S.W. should have on
her rolls, including women, just half-a-million
voters, and 70 per cent, of the whole population
of Australia will be Australian-born. The old
man said nothing about the original Kiama Ghost,
but trotted out the Chmese-invasion bogey,
George G. Turri, the clever patents agent
and expert, of Melb., is a perfect mine of infor-
mation on patent rights and laws throughout the
world. He was mainly instrumental in obtaining
the provision in the Vic. Patent Law for the very
necessary examination into novelty, inducing
Wrixon to insert the clause at the last minute.
Many an inventor has to thank Turri for the per-
sistent efforts he has been making for years to
protect genius against fraud. F. Harley Davis,
the inventor of the Calyx drill, the appliance
which is supplanting (he costly diamond drilling
plants, was a client of Turri’s, and obtained
<£2500 the other day for the right to use five
drills. “The Inventor's Guide,” by G. G. Turri,
is a sterling work on the subject of patents.
Toxa, the nonpareil rabbit - destroying
specific, is establishing wonderful records in the
Southern colonies, and circumstances are ap-
parently conspiring to banish all the old-fashioned
poisons in favor of this latest scientific prepara-
tion. The Melbourne press published a telegram
from Albury on 12th inst., that the devastating
fires which have recently swept through Riverina,
have been unmistakably traced to the spontaneous
ignition of the phosphorised preparations for
rabbit-poisoning, and a specific instance of the
destruction of 30,000 acres of grass is definitely
quoted. It is one of the special features of Toxa
that it is not inflammable, and cannot, in fact, be
set fire to; and it has been further proved that
cattle, horses and sheep will not eat Toxa, as they
will invariably eat phosphorised preparations of
wheat, pollard, etc. The National Wool Co. of
Australia Ltd., of Melbourne, are the agents for
Toxa in Australia and New Zealand.

THE ARGUS,
■SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 9, 1895.
Suicide is a subject at once unpic-
turesque and unpleasant, and the natural
impulse is to forget such a distressful
theme as expeditiously as possible. But
two casds of self-slaughter reported in
our columns during the week are of so
striking a character that they must
arrest general attention. In one case
ah Australian student in London, just
22 years of age, took his own life in
■apparently the most motiveless fashion,
and left behind him elaborate instruc- i
tions for deceiving everybody into the"’
belief that his. death was an accident.

Jane and Jessie Perkins, in South Mel-
bourne, is sure to oppress the mind with a
special horror. And yet it is but a drop in
the constantly flowing river of suicide, like
that of the youth Lloyd Burdekin, who, at
the age 0f.22. having exhausted his in-
genuity in order to dramatically act a lie
ae to the cause of his death, blew oat hU
brains, seemingly lor no other reason than
to try and cheat some coroner into return-
ing a verdict'of accident

Miss Micbie, the Yic. Q.C.’s daughter,
who orates on women’s suffrage in Melbourne,
says that the mistake women have made in the
past has been in worshipping man too much and
God too little. Miss Michie is mistaken. It is
precisely the other wav about.
» • *
The newly-formed Melb. female cycling-
club has decided to be sartorially irrational.
Though the raison d’etre of the club is woman’s
reasonable desire to show her legs, which are often
so much more fetching than her face, a rule has
been framed absolutely prohibiting fair members
from donning baggy breeches. It is understood
that the movers in this matter were some flat-
chested “ frumps ” with attenuated shanks, who
were fearful of incurring the ridicule of the street
urchin. One or two full-limbed girls strove boldly
for breeches, but were impotent before the power-
ful skirt coterie.
• • *
Dear Bulletin, —“ Why is it that man,
when he wants to look more than ususally impres-
sive, always gets into skirts?” In prehistoric
times scarcely anybody wore clothes who was not
powerful enough to rob some hunter, or was a
mighty hunter himself and annexed the hides from
the beasts of the field direct. Thus a big ward-
robe became synonymous with fame, power and
dignity, and as most folk wore their entire ward-
robe on their backs, the more togs a man wore the
bigger potentate he was thought to be. It’s only
a relic of out-of-date barbarism.—Yours, Y,
* * •
Miss Sutherland .(the opposition of the
Melb. Presbytery having made a New Woman of
her) says that “ man was never meant to rule the
world. That is woman’s mission. Man’s mission
is to work for woman.” But the lady has
generously admitted that woman couldn’t very
well get along without man. And yet a good
many have to—especially New Woman. There
were a few widows present on the platform when
the above ideas were enunciated —a coalition of
the new and secondhand, as it were.

The other day a young Victorian bride,
when the customary rice was thrown after her and
her male accomplice, turned back and broke her
umbrella over the head of the principal rice-
thrower, and had to be hauled off her mangled
victim before she could be induced to resume the
simple joys of the honeymoon. Yon can’t throw
rioe at the New Woman with impunity.
“ Is church-going a
perishing habit ? ” asks
Melb. Argus. The Bul-
letin thinks it is. Unless
ye repent ye perish, and
church-goers, as a rule,
become far too holy to
think of repenting in a
proper manner.

About one-third of
the police on Sunday
duty in Sydney decline
on principle to notice
infractions of the public-
house closing law. When
the thirsty people hover-
ing about a side entrance
become numerous one of
these Roberts magnani-
mously turns his back
and strides doggedly to
the opposite end of his
beat, to allow ingress
without detection.

An ingenuous little
maiden penetrated a
Sydney newspaper-office
recently and presented a
short notice of her
mother's last reception.
The notice not appearing
in ordinary course, she
made inquiries, and
learned that “only
leaders of society receive
notices gratis; when
tradespeople and shop-
keepers desire to have
their evenings at home
chronicled they are ex-
pected to pay cash per
inch.”
Governor Brassey, Victoria’s new vice-
gracious, pronounces his name “Bracy.” This is
the first step in the process by which Smith
became Carington, and Mullins became De
Moleyns, and “Duke Smithson of Northumber-
land ” became Percy. By-and-by, Brassey will
snell himself Bracy; then he will call himself De
Bracy which will probably be as near De Bressi
as he’ll care to go. Bracy has an old Norman
sound. Brassey is only vaguely suggestive of
the coarse old progenitor, king of the wheelbar-
rows who spoke of money<as “ brass,” and whose
coat of arms was a pick rampant. The enriched
Brassey or Mullins always developes a truly
Hinglish craving after a pedigree and a Norman
ancestor. Old Marshal Junot, who was as coarse
as any Brassey of the crowd, knew better. “I
don’t need ancestors,” he said when someone con-
doled with him on the obscurity of his family. 1
myself am an ancestor.”
N.9.W., long idle, has now promised his em-
ployes six weeks’ work. The Bulli miners are in
desperate straits. Scores of the unfortunate men
have of late been earning a few pence a day by pick-
ing blackberries, which grow wild in great pro-
fusion locally, and are now being extensively used
for jam.
One picture now at Hobart Exhibition
should certainly be secured by N.S. W. National
Gallery. “The Death of Brutus,” by Gustav
Wertheimer, represents a travelling showman and
his wife lamenting the death of their lion, whose
corpse has just been taken from its cage. It is a
splendid and pathetic picture. As the N.S. W. gal-
lery already possesses “ The Widower,” it might
be injudicious to buy “ The Bribe,” now shown at
Hobart Exhibition—a broadly but trickily-painted
picture, possessing human interest of the same
sort as “The Widower.” “The Bribe,” which
represents the father of a sick child dangling
before it a gaudy toy to induce it to take the
medicine offered by the mother, who kneels at
the other side of the bed, is full of clever light-
effects, and is a picture out of which a smart
showman with a talent for lying about how much
it cost him could easily make a little pile.
• * *
The Sydney “Churchman’s Alliance” last
week was discussing the best means of getting
more men to come to church. Teetotal Parson
Boyce advocated brightening the services (few
mented wine is a brightener, by the way !)
“mixing more with the men” by “evening visi-
tations ” (what penal blessings !) and joining male
“ societies.” Layman John Kent seemed to think,
that men should not be required to “ wash vip ”
and don “Sunday clothes.” Parson William
Martin condescendingly laid down the rule that
“ clergymen should converse more with the work-
ing men, who really like being talked to.” So
long as parsons adopt this posture of lofty
patronage towards the poor of their congregations,
so long will the obstinate and independent male
not only stay away from church, but consign al!
“ pastors and masters ” to —Heaven.

In the case of a Sydney “ daughter of joy,”
beak Addison advised her to be dealt with sum-
marily, instead of going to a jury “ Thechanoes
are that you will get two years there, whilst you
could only get six months here.” Lawyer Levien ;
“ But then, the chances are that she will get out.
I wouldn’t mind betting that she will.” Mr.
Addison: “I suppose a jury is more easily gob
round than I am.” ....!!! There are several
interesting aspects in which to consider this epi-
sode. There is the primarily paternal attitude of
the magistrate to the unfortunate girl, and his
free worldly advice. And there is afterwards his
undisguised contempt for the amorous impression-
ableness of the average juryman when confronted
with Lais with her hair down.

Two cases have, within the last week,
come before the N.S.W. courts of men who first
went to gaol in their boyhood for trivial offences,
and have been in gaol ever since. A slab from
one case: —
When it came to M'Guire’s turn, the gaol authorities
read out an appalling list of previous convictions. Al-
though quite a young man, the greater part of his life
has been passed in prison, with which he made his first
acquaintance when he was 15, and from which he was only
discharged last January. His last sentence was for
robbery with violence, and it was one of seven years and
two floggings. M'Guire urged, in extenuation, that he
had never known friends, education, nor kindness of any
sort, had never been taught a trade nor done a day’s work
in his life. What chance was there for such as he ? His
Honor sentenced him to seven years’ penal servitude.
“ The mixture as before !”

Williams, who killed his fellow-prisoner,
O’Connor, in that apparently disorganised insti-
tution, Bathurst Gaol, by hitting him on the head
with a broom, was tried at Sydney last week and
found guilty of manslaughter only. He was
excellently defended by Mr. R. D. Meagher,
Williams admitted the deed, bub pleaded great
provocation, and that O’Connor’s death was the
outcome of a blow in a sudden quarrel, and nob of
malice aforethought. The prisoner much improved
his case, in the eyes of the Court, by the evidence
he gave on oath on his own behalf. Judge Win-
deyer agreed with the verdict, and in sentencing
Williams to a year’s additional gaol, read him a
fatherly sorb of sermon. Williams is a lucky man.
From the manner in which his case had been
handled by the press apparently inspired by the
Bathurst gaol-officials he was regaled by out-
siders as a hopelessly bloodthirsty ruffian.
~ ' * . ~ , ~
Mr. Meagher, aforesaid, by the way, appeared
to defend his man in a gay suit with a magnificent
“button-hole’’under his legal nose. Wmdeyer
J. sent the lawyer a private note, that the solemn
occasion deserved a solemn suit, and adjourned
the court for an hour. At the end of which time
the advocate appeared in black and without the
bouquet. His Honor having snorted approval,
the case proceeded.

The N.S.W. Minister of Justice ought
really to ukase that no police-magistrate should,
under pain of dismissal, dine, or lunch, or drink
with anyone having a case before his court. Like-
wise, that no police-magistrate should constantly
drink at low public-houses with low attorneys—or
even occasionally fall down drunk in public places,
in or out of business hours. As police-magistrates
have to administer the murderous flogging-act,
and deal out lashes to such—haw—unbelltoppeerd
offenders against the person as appear before the
Bench, it is some what expedient that their Wor-
ships should all invariably be at least partially
sober.
N.S.W, Crown Law officers have decided
for purposes of marriage registration that a
divorced woman coming up for marriage may be
set down as “unmarried,” and, if she likes, she
may use her maiden name. It sometimes happens
that it is more convenient for her, on account of
children, to retain her late marriage name, and in
such cases her new marriage may take place under
that name with the designation “ unmarried ”
still attached. The Law officers were driven to
define these niceties owing to the bewildering
state in which registration papers have been
arriving from celebrants. Women were therein
described as to “condition” as “divorces,”
“divorcees,” “divorced from,” “divorced by,”
“widows,” “ex-wives,” “ legal widows,” “Act
of Parliament widows,” “ Windeyer widows,”
and many other sorts of curiosities.

The ordinary amateur Justice expects to
while away the morning only in playing the
Magistrate, and, if a case be lengthy, the unfor-
tunate defendant, whose defence is naturally last
and more diffuse, certainly do'es not receive fair
play. Another of. the advantages arising from
persons in business writing J.P. after iheir names.
However, some of the Stipendariea also have got
into the habit of working mornings only, and are
very nasty if compelled to expend brain tissue
after lunch.

A depressing spectacle of the time is a por-
trait of the principal actors in
a recent fashionable wedding,
on view in Collins-street.
The bride looks the beta,
but her bridegroom’s
knobby knees, kilted
in the most swagger
style, attract all
eyes from the con-
templation of her
graces. Spectators
think, with a sigh,
of the Sydney loan-
picture, “Wed-
ded,”-.in which the
young husband
shows twice as
much anatomy with
far more pleasing
effect. A modern kilt,
like an elbow-sleeve, dis-
plays the weakest point of
a limb, at the same time con-
cealing all extenuating circum-
stances.

(M.L.) church there
is a cross composed
entirely of bad
“silver” coins
piously dropped
into the collection-
plate. The cross is
very large and
handsome, and is
the product of only
two years’contributions. How
many other church ornaments
are made from money that an
honest man wouldn’t like to
touch?

“Why men don’t go to church.” One
reason is that, as a S.M. Heeald correspondent
puts it, “ Men aren’t women.” Another reason is
that the men have been to church.
• * «
“ Why men don’t go to church ” : Because
they mostly find in the pulpits the fools of the
family, the weaklings and the runts—the pink-
eyed rabbits and Italian greyhounds of humanity.
• * *
Apropos a recent Melb. tragedy. The
piano, remarks a Bulletin correspondent, is
always the last article which a Melbourne family
disposes of. They may eat their food off gin-cases
and sleep on the floor, but the piano they have
always with them, and the eldest daughter jingles
out “Dysy ” and other horrors, just to show the
neighbors that they haven’t reached that stage of
degradation and poverty known as “selling the
planner.” The yarn of the man who threw his
wife out of the second storey window of his burn-
ing house and carried the feather bed downstairs
in his arms, could not have been told of a Melb.
man, as he would have wanted his wife to help
him to carry down the piano.
* * •
The house where the S. Melb. double-
suicide took place is one of a row of about 20
wretched wooden cottages facing a triangular
reserve at the intersection of the St. Kilda and
Port Melb. railway lines. In the summer this
space is occupied by hundreds of children who use
it as a cricket-ground. It is all “made” ground
of river silt, and not a blade of grass appears on
its surface. In winter, a large portion of it is
covered wdth an inch or two of water, on the
top of which there floats a white slime, which fact
gives to this delectable region the name of Salt
Lake City. And yet people wonder at the
suicide.
* * *
Melb. Heeald howls weirdly because
there is no funeral service read over the bodies
consigned to the pickle-tub at Melb. University
for the use of the medical students. “Students
will cut and hack for six months more,” wails the
agonised editor, “ and shapeless human envelopes
will be committed to earth without Christian ser-
vice as of yore.” The Bulletin suggests that a
parson be engaged by contract to perform the ser-
vice once a week over the harness-cask in the
Medical School, after which the pickled Christians
might be buried without compunction when
worked up by the sucking sawboneses. The dead
vagrant, we notice, receives a deal more sympathy
than the living variety ; the latter is rarely gushed
AT THE MATCH.
Miss Ethel and her suitor spruce
Indulged in cricket chatting
The while big Harry Trott and Bruce
Were spiritedly batting.
Their names fell glibly from her lips
Whene’er her young adorer
Said, “ Trotty got it through the slips 1”
Or “Billy cut a fourer !”
She knew not t’other one from which,
Their figures oft mistaking—
She thought the bowlers wanted “ pitch ”
To keep the ball from “ breaking ” —
But like a darling girl she came,
Her heart with ardour ticking,
To see Australia win the game,
And England get a licking.
The score was what she understood :
Miss Ethel watched it mounting—
“ Trott’s 20 now,” she said. “ How good !
I wonder if he’s counting?”
Then fast across the level sward
They started off to run “ byes.”
Still gazing at the scoring-board
She cried, “ Which man is Sundries?"

A discussion has been
taking place in English
journals as to whether
girls should be married in
ignorance of the duties and
responsibilities of the posi-
tion, or whether the plain
facts should be placed be-
fore them. An English
High Church dignitary
gaveJt as his opinion that
“the Bible, with its chaste
descriptions ” —that is the
dignitary’s phrase—was
a sufficient introduction to
married life.”
For a series of “Scrip-
ture exercises ” for girls a
Victorian religious monthly
chose lately the book, of
Ezekiel, through which it
was necessary to go min-
utely to get the answers.
Ezekiel is about the most
obscene book in the Bible
—worse even than Leviti-
cus—-but its impurities are,
fortunately, of such an
outrageously diabolical
kind that they would be
as Greek to the average
girl.

Auckland (M.L.) Har-
bor Board docks war-
vessels of all nations at a
merelynominal price, while
merchantmen and even
mission vessels are made
to pay the full scale rate.
This is the same body that
secured the bringing into
operation of the. O.D. Act
in Auckland in the hope
that when the port was
thus rendered attractive to
seamen the naval head-
quarters for Australasia
would be established there.

* * *
Dr. Ahearne, at the Science Congress in
Brisbane, attacks the Q. educational regulations
asvbeing unfit for a tropical country—wherein Dr.
Ahearne is not far wrong. But Dr. Ahearne also
attacks the Queensland climate, as being unfit for
Europeans. Ansemia, fevers, dyspepsia, men-
strnal excesses, are the troubles of the Northern
clime, says the doctor. Undoubtedly, the climate
of the North was often fatal to the pioneer. But
the climate of nearly all newly-opened countries is
open to the same charge. Many Queensland towns
which had a terrible mortality 20 years ago are now
among the most salubrious in Australia. And if
the'Queensland climate be unsuited to the white,
statistics show it to be less well adapted to the
black, for the white race in the North is flourish-
ing and multiplying, where the sable and saffron
heathens are quietly rotting out of their slavish
existence.
• * #
The Church of England, at Temora
(N.S.W.), is threatened with the bum-bailiff,
and the poor parson didn’t have a square meal to
face even on Christmas Day. This is the story
told, and public pity is asked for. The philo-
sopher has no pity to bestow on such a case. The
bedrock idea at the root of mankind’s spiritual
yearnings, and even the alleged Christian’s pre-
tended ideal, are that “salvation is free,” and
that “ to the poor the gospel is preached.” Taking
these as beacon principles, it becomes a natural
conception of the most exalted kind that the shop-
herd who seeks to make a living out of the gospel
debases it and deceives both himself and his sup-
posed flock. The evangelist of the future will be
bke the primitive apostle—he will work with his
own hands and head at worldly craft to sustain
NOT IMPROMPTU.
MR. COCKATOO: “ Now, look here, Mr. Smith, do you practise
what you preach ? ”
REV SMITH: “ Yes—till I know it by heart.”
and maintain himself, and he will preach the
gospel “fornowt.” Just at present the salaried
parson is a huge satire on a Christianity that thus
provides Jehovah with a special civil service of
graded and degraded flunkeys. A parson who is
not content to take his chance with voluntary
contributions has no right to be a parson• he
ought to be a bill-discounter.
THE CORPSE-BEARER’S JOKE.
D RIVER (to near-sighted man, who has mistaken hearse for omnibus) : “All
right, sir, I’ll call for you later on !”
In Albany, Westralia, so it was elicited at a
Melb. police-court case last week, it is customary
for the gaol authorities to charge their lodgers on
release for board supplied.

Dear Bulletin, —Why not give the vege-
table, Chow a rest ? Goto market in Melbourne
any Wednesday or Saturday morning, and yon
will find hundreds of celestials stocking their
trucks from the wholesale European vendor. Go
to the.suburbs where Chinese gardens abound, and
the European retailer, if honest, will tell yon
that his shop is stocked by the neighbouring
Chows. This is the position, the Chow is polite
and punctual, and sells English-grown goods ; the
European fs not either, and sells Chow-grown
vegetables.—P. M,

it is irreverent to say that very few people are
brought to the Lord with the thermometer stand-
ing at 90 degrees.” Pub your sins on ice, young
man ; put ’em on ice.

Williams, the Bathurst (N.S. W.) convict
who killed his fellow-prisoner, O’Connor, had pre-
viously been flogged for destroying gaol furniture.
The lash acts as a potent deterrent. Also, in
Sydney, last week, a man who had been twice
flogged for garotting, came up and got another
sc' 1 ""'* years for robbery with violence.
# * *
It is averred that the two Stipendiary
Magistrates who revived the lash in Sydney, the
other day, actually, before the subsequently con-
victed offender was tried, had him, a legally inno-
cent man up to date, medically examined as to his
powers of physical endurance under a flogging.
In short, it would appear that the punishment
was, in a sense, prescribed before the formal hear-
ing < f the case.
* * *
A pleasing sign of the tfmes In a fashion-
able Melb. suburb—South Yarra —is a medical
man—retired surgeon-captain, Indian Army—who
charges half-crown fees for consultation. Why
not? Many an M.D. in London makes an honest
.£1000 a year by a daily round of visits at 3s. 6d.
each, and considers himself a fortunate fellow —
which, by the way, he is.
* * *
Tailoresses may now be seen on hot days
working under the shade of the trees in Carlton
Gardens, Melb. —the open air being preferable to
the stuffy bedroom, the only workroom of the un-
fortunate seamstress.
* * *
Gov. Hopetoun’s recent visit to Camper-
down (Vic.) inspired a melancholy local councillor
with poignant regrets. Never before had the
health of this venerable Rechabite been drunk in
strong drinks, and he shed tears over his fall from
grace, and afflicted the Governor and suite with a
gloomsome teetotal oration based on the fact that
he was 70 years old, and had never spent 6d. on
drink in his life. After which his lordship, very
much affected, proposed further healths, and
drank them in fizz. The man who never spent
6d. on liquor is, after all, not much worse than
the one who spends every 6d. in drink.

From S.M. Herald : —
Brisbane, Thursday.
Mr. Tozer, Colonial Secretary, has decided to declare
Monday, the 20th instant, a public holiday, instead of the
preceding Saturday, which is St. Patrick’s Day.
The brand of dimethylamidoazobenzosulphonio
acid that demoralises the almanac,, like this, that
shifts St. Patrick’s Day into February, that
knocks a Wednesday into Monday, a Sunday into
Saturday, and'the 18th into the 17t'h with a fresh
adjustment of the week all round—must be the
real old stingo, and ought to be passed round to
the other newspaper offices by way of brotherly
festival.
Neighbor : “ The piano fiend, cornet fiend,
banjo fiend, sewing-machine fiend, are ascending
scales of tor-r-ture, but what escels the pious
family, with the bumble-bee harmonium?”
Since the Wairarapa
disaster, great attention is
being paid to life-boat drill
on the various in ter provin-
cial steamers. Life - boat
drill at the best, however, is
a melancholy joke. Every-
body knows when it is going
to come off and turns up
calmly at his post. The
boats aren’t got out, so that
if the gear is rotten, or the
ropes won’t run, or the men
really don’t know how to
lower boats in a rough sea,
the defect isn't discovered.
The drill comes off in fine
weather and by
day, whereas the
frantic rush for
the boats in real
life generally
takes i lace on
a pitch-dark
night, when all
hell seems to
have broken
loose on the
waters. There
is no frenzied
scurry for the
life-belts, no
hysterical wo-
men wailing on
a deck that is
knee - deep in
water, and none
of the usual con-
comitants of a
wreck. Boat-
drill, on a
steamer which
is busy breaking
records for all
it is worth, is
always a farce, more or less, but the kind of drill
which is supplied is even more of a farce than it
need be.
MORE RETRENCHMENT.
These are retrenching times, and everywhere
One hears the bitter cry of men
Who had five quid on which to dress and fare,
Reduced, alas ! to two-pun ten.
The fall in prints, speakers understand,
For these reductions compensates,
And yet the outlawed babes at “Lloyd’s”
command
The old, inflated, boom-time rates ! ”
Melb. S.S.

sobriety.” That is the only thing in your letter worth
remembering K. (Melbourne): “All wealth comes
from the land ” is an axiom that mustn’t be taken too
literary. (1) Some wealth is acquired by fishing, then it
doesn’t come from the land but the sea. (2) Man can
produce little wealth in the first place without land, also
the land produces no wealth without man, therefore it is
quite as fair to say that all wealth comes from man. (3)
The value of most articles consists of something like one
per cent, value of raw material supplied by the land and
99 per cent, value added by labor and human intelligence.
(4) You may put it another way and say that all wealth
comes from water. Just try how much wealth you can
get from land where there is absolutely no water. (5)
Water comes from the atmosphere, therefore all wealth
comes from the atmosphere. (6) No one knows the origin
of the atmosphere, so no one knows where wealth comes
from E.B.B.: Telegraph offices may leak, but you

The firm of Crawford, King and Co.,
which has won such wide popularity as a trading
bouse in the Southern province during the past
five years, has, under the stress of very extenuating
circumstances, been forced into liquidation. It is
very unlikely that the goodwill of so sound a
business will be sacrificed, and it may be reason-
ably expected that when the present trouble is
over, the business will rise again bigger and better
than ever. Meanwhile, the trustee has his execu-
tive hand on the concern, and the purport of these
presents is to intimate that the large and valuable
stock is being sold at a great sacrifice. A few
good lines in men’s necessaries are referred to by
advt. elsewhere.
