SPORTING NOTIONS.
Amongst the Newmarket light-weights, Esau —
Sep. Miller's —looks like having a chance. He started at
Aspendale Park the other day and won two rac es, beating
fairly good fields, and on the following Tuesday won the
Mentone Flying Handicap, and ran a dead-beat later on.
Practically four wins in as many starts. He carries 6 - 9
in the big event t The fancy top-weight, Whakawatea,
has also shown form, winning at Cootaniundra in a canter
with 10-7. But the quality of the field must be con-
sidered.
♦ • * • •
lb is unfair to Byron Moore for the cricket
authorities to clash with the Newmarket. As the New-
market happens regularly every year, its fixtures should
be respected. As things are, most racing people will see
the Newmarket on Saturday and the match on Monday.
• ♦ * •
Byron Moore, on behalf of the V.R.0., en-
deavored to get a postponement of the Mclh. “rubber"
match until after his club’s forthcoming mec ting, but the
M.C.C. couldn't entertain the idea. Thedat.es were pro-
bably fixed with the express purpose of giving the teams
a chance to get the Thursday—if not the Tuesday also —
at Flemington after finishing their tussle.
* » * •
Dear Bulletin, —Re favourites’ chances in the
Newmarket, you remark: “But how seldom do early
favourites win big events?" I wish to ask, on the con-
trary, how often does an early favourite—afterwards
neglected for some flash-in-the-pan— turn up and win,
causing innumerable exclamations of: “’Slrew'th, why
didn’t I stick to him 1” After years of close observation,
I conclude that the favourite on the day of the race sel-
dom wins. For his Melb. Cup, Malvolio was an early
favourite, while last year Patron was favourite s r me time
before the race, hut started at long odds. So, also, Paris
for Caulfield Cup.—Walter Stock.
« * • •
A straight-out tip for the Newmarket Handi-
cap is Whakawatea, and nothing else. A Maonlander
won it last year in Hova, and Lochiel won it previously,
when he just pipped Abner. Then Whakawatea is the
fastest sprinter in Maoriland, and may win from end to
end.
• » * •
The reason why Humphrey Oxenham takes to
promoting consultations is not far to seek. From the
way legitimate bookmaking is running down he knows
that within, say, three years, there won’t be any ante-
post betting, and very little, and precarious at that, in
the paddock. There is even now no real bookmaking—
no getting round and laying to your book. There are
never more than three horses backed at suburban races,
and not more than six in a race at Randwick, and, as a
consequence, it is the layers who plunge, not the punters.
The public are frequently right, too, so that when a
live ’tin gets home the “books” have not tak> n enough
to pay their losses. If any reader doubts this let him
count up and see how many of the sound men of seven
to two years ago, are to be seen with the bag on, nowa-
days.
• • * •
An interesting comment on racing “form,” as
shown at the little outside meetings, was published in
a Vic. daily last week. The paragraph put the case
rather delicately:—" , another Newmarket candidate,
started in the Handicap. Although he finished out of a
place, his supporters for the Flemington event were very
well satisfied with his running.” On pai tr there was
much cause for the horse’s supporters to tear their hair.
* ♦ • •
Now that Carbine has departed, how long is
Australia to retain his illustrious rival, Abercorn ? As far
as good looks and breeding go, the latter suffers nothing
from comparison. In w eight-for-age races, it was almost
a dead-heat between them.
* • * *
Said that Carbine was offered at 15,000 guineas
to Haggin, the American sportsman, who declined to buy
at the price.
* • • *
After all, Carbine didn’t go so very cheap.
Taking freight, insurance, &0., into account, he will have
cost Jumbo Portland nearly ihrioe as much as Sam Hor-
den gave for Nordenfeldt, as well-bred, as good-looking,
and almost as good a performer.
• • • *
Musket’s first son in Maoriland was Fnider, the
most arrant of duffers. The writer was abusing him one
day when he was pretending to race at Ellerslie, and
Bennett, now owner of Euroka but then a director
of the M.L. Stud Co., chipped in with, “Yes, curse him ;
if that brute had never been foaled our company would
be thousands richer to-day.” And there was truth in the
remark. Snider depreciated the first crop to an inestim-
able extent. They were all Muskets right enough, but
till James White bought Martini-Henry they wouldn’t
go off!
• • • •
Spagnoletti: “ I have just been astounded out-
side Spenoer-st. Railway-station, Melb. A small boy, per-
haps 14, came up, and wanted to know if I would hack a
good double for Mentone. He had a complete printed
threepenny betting-book, with a lot of the stumps where
he had laid wagers.”
• • • ♦
At Avenel (Vic.), the other day, Arthur Bayley,
of Bayley’s Reward, ran his old pack-horse, Coolgardie
Ben, against a local crack, Reporter, for £lOO aside. The
latter, which had won many races, was looked on as a
dead cert., and a big crowd assembled to see the old ’un
put down. There was plenty money going, and Bayley
took all he could get. The horses kept together for a
mile and a-half, when Reporter was under the whip, but
to no purpose, as “ the packer” came away and won a
splendid race by half-a-length, to the great disgust of the
ring. Ben is a chestnut, is about 15’2, and apparently
12 or 13 years old. He is said to be identical with a once
well-known Vic. racer shipped years ago to Westralia.
• * • *
The unvarying fidelity of the jockey-boy to his
employers in roping bids fair to become a thing of the
past. As a rule, when asked to explain, the jockey
always insisted that his orders were to “getaway well
and win if you can.” The other day, however, at some
Melb. pony-races, a rider, when asked the reason of his
evident anxiety to lose, declared that he had ridden as
instructed. Pony and owner were rubbed out, while the
boy was dismissed, with a caution. He was waited on,
however, by one or two of the “push” and forcibly
argued with. * * »
Dear Bulletin, —That Carbine picture was a
cruel blow. For years I have been dodging that white
near hind leg. It has driven me to do my own shaving,
and to be a teetotaller—so far as pubs, are concerned.
Only two hotels in Melbourne have not got the picture
hung up in some shape, and they are not on my beat.
Ever since the proposed sale of tfm beast was announced
I have abstained from buying ordinary newspapers that
were bound to reproduce the wi etched animal. I have
sworn off sport until he has been dead 50 years. English
papers will now soon be also closed to me. Gradually my
life is being denuded of every enjoyment. I have even
had to drop church and the religious journals to avoid
statistics as to how many loaves of bread might he bought
with £13,650. And now you deal the final bl< w. Tell me
straight, do you intend to reproduce a picture of Car-
bine’s first “get” on the other side? Do you intend to
reproduce that accursed white leg etc., when the horse
dies, or when its owner dies, or upon any other feeble
occasion ? If so, give me due notice, or The Bulletin
and I must part for ever.—Yours, Cop.

A small sporting news-sheet, published in Mel-
bourne, gives away the fact that it is usual for racing
clubs to provide “members of the fourth islate” with
refreshments. And, whereas the Geelong club official
didn’t “dole out ” luncheon tickets to the reporters until
the victuals had been mostly devoured, the representative
of the rag aforesaid paid 2s. for his own tucker and felt
grossly insulted. The self-respect of a pressman who
expects to be fed gratia by ttie mauageuic nt when he
writes a paragraph about a show is a very delicate thing,
and very easily wounded, apparently. Also, his habit of
exposing a bleeding spirit to the world is doosid affecting.

The Y.R.C. stewards marched slowly and
solemnly round Fiemington course one day last week.
After doing the full distance they announced that certain
rumors as to the roughness, unevenness, and brick-like
character of the turf were founded on fiction.

Wicket-keeper Jarvis is very pleased at his
success in the big matches, more especially as he was evt n
discarded by S.A. selectors last stason. The S.A. team
against Victoria was picked before the Australians re-
turned from America, and M’Kenzie was substituted for
Jarvis, but the former being injured before the match,
Jarvis was picked and gave a brilliant exhibition. S.A.
cricketers aver that he was never in such fettle as he is
now.

It is thought in N.S.W. sporting circles that
the coming session of Parliament will pass a Bill to sup-
press the tote-shops. Last week a big sporting man
leased some large Sydney premises with ihe proviso that
the lease should immediately terminate should legislative
enactment interfere with the business.
•-• * •
Swimming is the latest boom in Melbourne.
Melbourne Swimming Club had a “ladies’ day” at St.
Hilda baths recently. The competitors were all decorously
clad from neck to knee, and were “6 tnafide amateurs.”
A grand swimmer appeared in Harry Heath, the club’s
captain, who won the 300 yards from scratch, conceding
as much as GOsecs. to the limit men. Heath has a rapid
stroke and an extremely powerful kick.

Logan, the M.L. yacht-builder—he constructed
the Akarana and other cracks known in Australia —re-
cently built a yacht to the order of a syndicate of Welling-
tonians, the proviso being that it was to beat the Lyttel-
ton champion or no sale. This his boat did handsomely
by 30 minutes. The price of the yacht was £740.

* * « •
A Melb. Civil servant has invented a bicycle
which, it is predicted, will revolutionise cycling. In the
new machine the rider sits directly over the pedals, and,
by an ingenious method of gearing and leverage, is en-
aided to use all his weight, besides getting a powi r
hack-kick, which is wasted in the present style of machine.
The hands are also brought into use, the action being
said to resemble that of walking. The inventor is being
backed by some experts who seldom make an error, ai d
patents have been secured throughout the world. Sixty
miles an hour(!) is the rate of speed expected from an
average rider on an ordinary road, while a professional
will give Fortunatus half way and a licking over six fur-
longs.

The defeat of Maorilander Sullivan by the
Englishman, Harding, may give rowing the fillip which it
badly needs. Harding has carried all before him in
England, a performance, however, which will not stand
much bragging about during the past decade or two.
Sullivan has lately been keeping a public-house, which
would not enhance his chances.

Corbett calls Steve O’Donnell the cleverest

three hours he was in the ring with Peter Jackson. Peter
taught Steve an he knows, and can now teach him a good
deal more.

A writer in the Clarion (Eng.) scoffs at the
trash which, as he evidently is not aware, gets cabled to
Australia as E iglish crick't criticism. “Who,” he asks,
“is the invenlive pressman garnishing the reports on this
side of the water ? If the long description of the game
(first test-match 1 which we read in last week’s p ipers are
true reports from Australia, what are we to think of the
gentleman who laid great stress on the fact that M’Laren
and Stoddart a’tered their usual style of baiting to that
of the barn door order, so that the Englishmen might
make a draw of the game? The reporters on the Sydney
side knew that the match would be played out, and this
makes me look with dubious eyes on the descriptions of
the game which appeared in the papers over here.”

Coningham seems to have lost his temper in
the Brisbane mitoh. In some suspects he is a model,

self, neither drinks nor smokes—for he has a nasty habit
of biting his thumb till the blood comes if he feels bad
about anything.

It is an established fact that nicotine is
injurious to health, sufficient being contained in
an ordinary cigarette or pipeful of tobacco to
poison a dog. Does it not, therefore, become a
duty to every man to smoke an article that does
not contain nicotine, yet retains all the essential
qna'ities of a fragrant cigar ? Frossard’s Cavour
cigars have the nicotine extracted in vacuo, and
are recommended by the highest authorities as
non injurious. They are sold everywhere in
packets of eight for Is.
At the close of ’93, when the Mutual Life
Insurance Co. of New York totted up accounts, it
found t iat its assets had been increased by about
.£2,0 0,000, its surplus by about <£500,000, and its
income by some .£300,000. At the close of last
year, as the Australian general manager (Major
Rennie) has just learned by special cable, the in-
crease of assets over those of ’93 was more
than .£3,500,000, of surplus nearly .£1,000,000,
and of income <£1,200,000. The figures as they

Wc are prepared to supply Agricultural Implements at prices hitherto unequalled in Queensland
Australia, chewing much meat with false
teeth, is not uninterested in the outward visible
appearance of Edwin Lenthall Oldfield, of Mel-
an accomplished dentist and quite famous
tenor singer. His voice has nothing to do with
the publication of his photo., in this instance.
Mr. Oldfield is figuring just now as the anxious
father of a Dental College and Oral Hospital,
which will also be, according to terms of registra-
tion, “ a charitable institution for the admission,
as in or out patients, of the deserving poor.”
Prior to his election to the conservative Dental
Board of Victoria, this live practitioner sug-
gested, by circular, the scheme he has now
taken in hand. A school of dentistry is
needed in every big city of Australia.
Speaking for his own province, and for
Melbourne in particular, Mr. Oldfield waxes
eloquent upon the theme. Until the year 1887
there was no protection for qualified dentists in
Victoria. Anybody could furnish a small room
with an operating-chair, hang out a colored lamp.
Photo, by [?]] [Melbourne.
EDWIN LENT HALL OLDFIELD.
and invite the unwary to have first aids to diges-
tion pulled out by the roots. In ’B7 the Dental
Board started with a slop-made Act of Parlia-
ment at its back, and a eertif. of efficiency from
the examining body became the recognised card
of introduction to the profession. Students have
had the University and School of Pharmacy
whereat to run their educational coarse, but the
idea of a Dental College, with clinics, post-
graduate classes, &c., was left for an individual
dentist to introduce. Naturally, Oldfield’s action
has excited snorting comments in certain quarters,
although his claim to do this good thing
for the general good, as well as his own im-
mediate advantage, is beyond question. Standing
in the front rank of his profession, and being
specially esteemed by the medical fraternity, ho
appeared the right man for the work; and, again,
he is in a position to make a success of it. Mr.
Oldfield, some time ago, secured a lease of the
house adjoining his well-known residence, 36
Nicholson-street, Melbourne, and number 34 will
be formally opened, anon, as the Dental College
and Hospital aforementioned. Two gentlemen of
high attainments in the dental and medical pro-
fessions are associated with the founder, so the
College will have a sufficient staff of masters at
the outset. The idea “caught on” as soon as it
came before the public. Mr. Oldfield has already
several students entered for the course of four
years’ training necessary to a pass at the Board’s
exam. Moreover, two or three ladies promptly
tripped into the new college for a twelvemonth
course of tuition in the art of “filling.”
Lovely woman is an important person in
American dentistry. Mr. Oldfield expects to turn
her to as much account here. The feminine hand
deft and steady—not, as a rule, shaken by over-
nightwhiskies-and-soda—and a properly-instructed
vision in an undivided skirt may put gold or silver
into a cavernous tooth as neatly as the best of the
baser animals. Even if the Dental College of
Victoria had no other purpose than the education
of ladies in this branch of the profession it would
fib a long-felt want. The thought of a golden-
haired girl dropping precious metal into one’s
“ toof ” is exceedingly appetising.
At Aden, the water for the poor has
usually to be fetched 28 miles on camel-back. A
lady there lost an ear-ring. It was mysteriously
recovered in possession of an Arab, which led to
the disclosure that her bath-water had been sold
by a servant for poor people to drink.
Melb. Argus remarks of Hon. W. P.
Reeves, of Maorilund, that he “seems to have had
a smattering of education in early life.” Reeves
is a University man, an excellent scholar and no
mean poet. His smattering of education isn’t the
same sort of smattering that the Argus is smat-
tered with. Which makes the Argus wild.
Nothing but dread of the cant-brigade
prevents at least two Sydney daily papers from
bringing out a Sunday edition after the American
fashion.
A new German law decrees that any person
who, either publicly or privately, “condemns
monarchy, marriage, the family, or the institution
of property ” shall be liable to three years’im-
prisonment. The new German revolution should
be getting almost in sight now.
Sarah Nash, of Coburg (Vic.),
suicide the other day by forcing down
her throat. She was interrupted before she could
open it.
“ f despise an editor,” says.Parkes, “ who
puts one thing in a leading article and another
thing in another column and paragraphs hgre and
there —all irreconcilable.” The>editor charged
witli these indecencies was once Parkes’ most
zealous barracker.

320 C-^UVUZEUiS
320 CAMELS
GREAT SHIPMENT OF
CAMELSI
JUST ARRIVED.
Now on View near Perth .
Pack & Biding Camels of the best Breed,
From
I- LIARAN,
BELUOHISTAN, and
AFGHANISTAN.
Most reliable for carrying and travelling purposes,
unsurpassed by any hitherto imported from India
—real beauties.
These camels are now at Lime Cliff Paddock,
about miles from Subiaco and 3 miles from
Claremont, where they will remain for three
weeks to undergo the necessary quarantine regu-
lations.
They are undergoing one month’s quarantine,
during which period they will be fed on good,
strengthening food, and no expense will be spared.
These are not Calcutta camels—or, rathei, those
that are known as such—but the real Hill breed ;
accustomed to feed on bushes and grass identical
with those of Western Australia; as only used by
the Army Commissariat Department of India,
consequently they can be thoroughly recom-
mended. They are directly shipped from Kurra-
chee, the nearest seaport to Kbaran, Beluchistan
and Afghanistan.
The s.s. Claverley, a first-class steamer, giving
comfortable accommodation to 400 camels, was
chartered for the special purpose of bringing from
Kurrachee these 320 camels. All these camels are
in good condition, as we have taken great trouble,
no expense having been spared by Messrs. D. and
J. Fowler on our behalf.
Diggers and JProspectors are
invited to deal at once.
All telegrams and letters should be addressed
to me at “ Criterion Hotel, Perth,” until further
notice.
For prices and full particulars address—
Capt. 0. DOUGLAS SAUNDERS,
Manager;
SULTAN RAZ MAHOMED,
7 Victoria Buildings, Coolgardie, W.A.
Bankers: ... Union Bank, Coolgardie.
N.B. —As these camels are from a cool part of
the East, they will by the time they leave quaran-
tine be in magnificent condition. I insert this as
Indian camels are considered useless by some of
the judges in Coolgardie. This may apply to the
Calcutta camels, but cannot be taken as applying
to Kbaran camels.

WHEREAS the Commissioners of Fisheries
tor New South Wales have reported that
the natural supply of fish in Port Stephens has
become exhausted to such a degree as to require
rest for the recovery thereof: Now, therefore, I,
Sir Robert William Duff, the Governor of the
Colony of Ifew South Wales, in pursuance of the
Government Notices.
provisions of the Fisheries Act, 1881, and with the
advice of the Exeeutive Council, do, by this my
Proclamation, declare that the several portions of
that water specified in the Schedule hereto
appended shall be closed against the use of fishing-
nets for a period of two years from the date
hereof.
Given under my Hand and Seal, at Government
House, Sydney, this 4th day of February, in
the year of our Lord one thousand eight hun-
dred and ninety-five, and in the fifty-eighth
year of Her Majesty’s Reign.
By His Excellency’s Command,

OTIOE OF FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT*
In the matter of the application of Herman Henry Groth and Cecilia Alice Pfahlert, mortgagees under
memorandum of mortgage from Rudolph te Kloot, No. 139,366, dated June 17, 1887, over the
whole of the land comprised in Crown Grant, vol. 879, folio 107.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagees have made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until 29th March, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which I, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants .an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under him,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
gd
A Plan and further particular* may bo obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Eliza*
beth-street, Sydney.

‘A GOLDEN
Mr. Fletcher Watson, the popular water-
oolor arttst, lately of' Sydney, writes that he is
progressing fast in England, and has been elected
a member of many societies. He' is represented
in the Academy this year.
A digester, a huge boiler containing 200
carcases of sheep, exploded at a Deniliquin boii-
ing-dbwn works the other day, and distributed
about two tons of stewed mutton over the district.
The oldest inhabitant was heard to remark that
he never before witnessed such a sud'den rise and
fall in mutton. The dogs of Deniliquin have
since developed quite a religious strain, and sit
on their tails, looking heavenward with the devo-
tion of saints.
A Rangiora (M.L.) publican recently said
a glass of beer to a travelling saint on a bicycle,
w f ho thereupon laid an information against him,
for ‘‘supplying a man who was already under the
influence of liquor.” The publican’s defence was
that the cyclist showed no signs of liquor, but the
man on the spidery wheel swore that he was drunk,
and pointed out that he was in a better position
to know that he was drunk than the publican to
know that he wasn’t. The court finally decided
that a man was the best judge of his own drunk-
enness, and fined the miserable grog-merchan'
10s.
The Colonial Sugar Co. and Queensland
planters generally look with dislike and fear at
the increase in the number of co-operative mills
which are threatening to compete for their profits.
The Sugar Co. has once more substantially reduced
the price of sugar, doubtless partly with the view
of frightening off small capitalists ; and an agita-
tion is now proceeding to hinder further Govern-
ment subsidies from being granted to co-operators,
on the ground that the export margin will shortly
be reached and the trade made profitless. Bluff 1
Conan Doyle, the physician and novelist,
thinks that brain-paralysis will be the horror of
the future. - Scientists are learning how to tackle
almost all the especially fatal diseases of the pre-
sent and the past, but no one has made any pro-
gress worth speaking of in dealing with paralysis,
which keeps steadily increasing as other horrors
diminish. Paralysis is the disease that you get
through long and agonising research after ways of
curing other diseases. It comes on the man who
lives in a rush all the days of his life to get out a
paper, and the man who has a diseased bank
weighing on his mind, and the man who totters
under too much dead weight of learning, and the
man who invents new things and new ideas, and
the man who hustles generally. Nobody knows
exactly what causes it, but it seems to arise from too
much hustling as often as from any other cause.
Paraflysis is one of the consequences of success, and
what shall It profit a man if he discovers a dozen
entirely new microbes, or gets his paper to press in
good time every night for 20 years, when at last
he finds his speech beginning to get thick, and ho.
has to stop every now and then because lie can’t
find the word that he wants, and he has a sensa-
tion as if the top ofdiis head was lighter than the
lower part of it, and his tongue has an enlarged
and helpless feeling in ins mouth, and lie begins
to write “ by sound,” so that when he wants to
set down “ beginning ” he, as likely as not, writes
“ gehenna” simply because the two words have a
faint resemblance to one another? Then if he
has any one dependent upon him, about the only
thing that is left for him to do is to insure his
life as often as possible, and to appomt a peepect-
able trustees co. as his executors, and sell his
bank shares for whatever they will fetch to save
his widow from calls. And after that he can lie
back and reflect that he is going to end ins days
as a patient idiot smiling at all the world, with
an attendant to wash him because he won’t know
how to wash himself,..and to prevent him wander-
ing peacefully into the dust-bin, and to bring him
in out of the wet.
Sydney “In Memoriam” notice:-
Farewell, dearest mother, from us thou art gone;
cold hand hath snatched thee away.
Thy pain and thy trouble now end in the tomb ;
For in peace you are suffered to lay.
We have often wondered where the high-toned
boarding-house eggs came from.

When buying The Bulletin at any wharf
or railway - station, make sure, before paying,
that you get the current issue, as a number of
people have been making money by trading off
ancient copies on the unsuspecting.
When a Salvation Army lass died recently
in a M.L. town, the local paper innocently came
out with this: “The funeral is noticeable as the
first Salvation one that has occurred in this town.
Let us hope it will not be the last.”
At a meeting of the Science Association in
Brisbane, it was stated in a paper by a member
that slaves are transhipped at Singapore and sent
off to Java and Borneo—aforesaid proceedings
taking place under the supervision and surveil-
lance of British naval authorities.
W. Errington, C.E., who constructed the
first locomotive ever built in Victoria, died the
other day in Auckland, M.L. Errington designed
t le two graving-docks in Auckland Harbor, one
of which, said to be the deepest in the world, is
now used chiefly as a swimming-bath.
On Queensland communal settlements the
cost of living has certainly attained the irreducible
minimum. Under the care of a paternal Govern-
ment it is down to Is. per week per man.
The minimum for paupers “in English workhouses
is put at 2s. 6d. per head per week.
Northern sugar-lands are wonderfully rich.
Land on the Burnett has produced cane for nearly
20 years in succession. Innisfail, on the John-
stone, has raised cane for 13 consecutive years.
Last year the land lay fallow, and the crop this
year is rich enough to have sprubg from virgin
soil.
The business of the suburban police-court
o Esse idon (Melb.) had to be adjourned the other
day, because the chairman only turned up, and
the police failed to find a second beak in any of
the “ likely places.” The same trouble couldn’t
ar.se in connection with a. certain court nearer the
heart of Melbourne, whose magistrates, or some
of them, attend “likely places” at given times for
the purpose of receiving likely visitors.
A wife giving evidence lately in a main-
tenance-case tried in a N.S. W. police-court, stated
that she told her mother-in-law, when she was
beating one of her (the wife’s) children, that the
child was not to be knocked about, as “ it was of
honorable birth.” “ And why did you say that ?”
asked defendant’s solicitor. “ Because it was
more than my husband could boast of,” was the
reply. A neat and effectual way of making things
sultry for husband and mother-in-law in one act.

A Maoriland inventor named Jones lately
discovered an instantaneous and infallible cure for
burns and scalds. He got together an audience of
about 50 people in a paddock at Mas ter ton, and
after lecturing on his antidote he dipped his hand
into a pot of boiling water. Then amid his howls
of anguish he applied the remedy, but it didn’t
produce any effect, and three medical men had to
hold him down and dress his injuries. Strangely
enough the badly-scalded Jones still believes in
his discovery, and is going to give another exhibi-
tion. True faith endures even though boiling-
w iter is thrown over it.

“Her Majesty the Queen is being urged
to try the new treatment for reducing weight. As
is well known, the Queen has almost entirely lost
the power of walking, owing, to a great extent, to
ley increasing stoutness, and it is thought that, if
thi ■ could be reduced, she would regain the use of
her limbs. The necessary regimen has nothing in
c 'inmon with the ordinary Banting or dietetic
c lies, but consists merely in taking a certain
animal preparation in the form of tabloids. In
the case of the Prince of Wales, the treatment has
been successful H.R.H. having, it is said, got rid
of 2it. of his adipose tissue in the space of a fort-
night.’ In plain English : “ Queen Victoria, now
too fat to walk, will try thyroid tablets, which
took 2st. off Albert Edward in two weeks.” Always
condense when writing to The Bulletin.

Of this amount £2,500,000 can be accounted for by the
increased Bank holdings. The remaining, say, £'3,100,000
may be treated as the nminal “disappearance” in 16
years. Much of this would be taken away by passengers
to other provinces and to foreign parts, some would go
into ordinary ■< insulation, and some would be privately-
melted by jewellers, &c., &c. It must be recollected,
however, that the ordinary shipments by banks, shipping
agents, Chinese merchants, and so on, are accounted for
among the exports.
As regards the 1889-94 period, an amount of
£1,750,000 is accounted for by the increase that has
occurred in the bank cash reserves. An allowance should
also he made for increase in the circulation of sovereigns,
consequent on the unpopularity of bank-notes since the
crisis. It is also douhthss true that during the crisis,
and during the exodus to Westralia, specially large
amounls were carried away by passengers (in th"ir
luggage, in lieu of drafts. With due consideration for
such deductions, the private hoards may' now be cal-
culated.

The amount of Victorian private hoardings at the
present moment, then fore, reaches the extraordinary
amount of £2,600,000 —and may well strike awe into
the soul of him who speaks of “ returning confidence.”
Very little of this hoarding appears to belong to ’94—it. is
carried overfrom ’93—and it may he seriously doubted that
any of the hoards will be return'd to the present banks
in til" present condition of affairs. The total reserves of
coin a- d bullion held by Vic. banks on 31st Dec. last were
£8,337,959, being abnormally large as some preparation
against surprise—but the private hoardings amount to 31
percent, of this sum! It is stated that the Australian
banks have to find £35,000,000 before the end of the cen-
tury, and in the interval before the State Bank comes, or,
at any rate, till the thirty-five millions are paid, it may
be safely anticipated that hoardings will increase.
« » * *
Australasia’s two land-grant railways—the Mid-
land • *f Westralia and the other Midland which Stout and
Vogel fixed on the unfortunate population of Maoriland—
continue to muddle along wretchedly to very little pur-
pose. The Westralian Co. is now floating a third loan by
way of mortgage on its little streak of unfinished rails;
the first loan was guaranteed by the W.A. Government,
and the other ranked as a second mortgage on the pro-
perty, but the new one is now to be considered the second
mortgage, and the old second mortgage becomes a third
lien on the co.’s rather shadowy security. Most of the
time and the spare cash of the Westralian Midland O.
seem now to be spent in operations for raising more
loans, and the line itself makes very slow and expensive
progress. The Midland of Maoriland, after long negotia-
tions, many bitter quarrels, and many attempts to induce
the Govt, to guarantee its loans, or to hand over land
before the work specified in the contract is accomplished,
has now, by virtue of a clause in the agreement, gone to
arbitration. A huge bill for law-expenses is therefore in-
evitable, and if the award goes against the Government it
will probably cost Maoriland more to leave the railway-
unbuilt than it would have cost to build it in the ordinary
fashion. The Midland Oo., of Maoriland, did very little of
the work it promised to do, and got various small con-
cessions in the early days of the enterprise, but it con-
tinued to leave the work undone and to ask for more con-
cessions until Parliament decided that it wanted the
earth, and swore-off future negotiations. The W.A.
Parliament will also come to the swearing-off stage by and
by. These two experiments with the land-grant railway
curse should satisfy the people of Australasia for all time.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
Many sad or romantic sequels have followed the
Wairarapa disaster, but until now no one thought that it
would cause the wreck of a big business concern. One
of the saved passengers was insured in a certain
Accident Insurance Co. His claim, £20 clrl, for injuries,
medical attendance, &c., was admitted by the co., which,
on application, paid £5 on account. Nothing could extract
the balance —indeed, the co.’s manager expressed disgust
at pressure being exercised for the balance, inasmuch as
(so he said) the claimant “ wasn’t hard-up, as he had seen
him in the street dressed in the height of fashion, wearing
a gold chain and tan boots.” A writ was issued, served,
and judgment got, but the execution was defeated on the
ground that there was nothing seizable in the office, there
being £600 owing for rent alone. If half the co.’s liabili-
ties were to mature in 12 months it would be liable for
a million.
* * * *
A Vic. hotelkeeper, whose good nature brought
him into insolvency, staggered the newly-fledged lawyer
(who accused him,during bankiup toy examination, of avoid-
ing service of a writ) by retorting: “ You must be very
young, my dear sir, and very green at your business, if
you don’t know that for 12 months prior to my insolvency
I specially set Mondays apart for the reception and
recording of writs, summonses, and the like, and that on
no other day could 1 devote time to the tremendous rush
of business with which exjei ienced solicitors invariably
favored me on that particular day.” He had had 137
writs and over 100 summonses in one year.
* * * *
American and Australian life-assurance offices
are now very close in their advances to canvassers. It is
said that one big office advanced £3000 to one cam asser,
who soon departed, but he certainly had done splendid
work previously. From £10 to £100 is now the limit, and
big biz. has to be shown for that.

Some day Australia will wake up with a pain in
its Treasury chest to find that it has been done once more
—by stamp-forgers this time. Sheets of stamps will
arrive from parts unknown, and will not be marked
“ made in Germany but in a quiet retail way a compact
gang will serve out those stamps to the beastly public,
and postal conferences will sit talking all the while. This
may happen this week, or next week. Can anyone say il
it is not happening now ?

N.S.W. law-courts are unsettled as to what
constitutes a fair rate of interest. One of them allowed
6 per cent., the other day, but only a month or so ago
another gave judgment with 8 per cent., while two or
three Judges think 5 and eVen 4 per cent, enough.
* ♦ * *
Messrs. Pitt, Son, and Badgery, of Sydney,
furrjish an interesting return showing the sources of
Britain’s meat-supply. In 1881, Australia furnished a J I
the frozen mutton sent to England. In ’B2 Maoriland
chipped.in, and last year sent over two million carcases,
equal to half the total supply. Australia, with five times
as many sheep, sends only 900,000 carcases. Queensland
has made a vigorous grab for the frozen beef trade, and,
though it only started in ’B9, it sent 15,000 tons last year,

That gorgeous specimen “from the Shamrock,
Murchison,” exhibited in Collins-st., Melb., was the im-

♦ ♦ * *
Under the present competitive system wages
filter through the carbon of necessity, and there is no
bedrock except zero. Some portions of the contracts for
N.S.W. Government buildings have been sub-let over and
over again to the fourth starving contractor, until the
wages paid to carpenters were from 2s. to 2s. 6d. per day.
Thus £lOO worth of contract-work would be sub-let by A
to B for £BO ; from B to C for £6O ; from O to D for £5O ;
and from Dto E for £4O. E has to make £4O pay him for
his material, his time, hia paid labor and his own profit.
So he roots among the “ scab ” carpenters and pays them
2s. a day each, instead of 10s. or 12s. 6d., and keeps them
up to the mark as far as he can by working alongside of
them and “shouting” an occasional beer. All the
“hidden” work is scamped, while the outside makes a
fair show. If the £4O job doesn’t pan out as he expected
—E “slips up” his men for the balance of wages due,
and either absents himself from his haunts, or goes in-
solvent, or “gets robbed,” or has a “sick wife” and
“doctor’s bills,” or anything. D makes £lO without
putting a tap to the work, and with the chance of dodging
E without even paying him, if it suits his hook ; O makes
£lO, with similar side-ohanoes ; B makes £2O and A £2O.
Sid. Gregory facing Richardson.
Gregory's Late Gut.
All, ultimately, out of the beer-canny workman who has
to take whatever wage he can get.

back the Duff Dibbs correspondence demanded by Parlia-
ment.” How often per parliamentary day is this sort of
thing done ? About 1887, Gillies being then Premier, &0.,
the Vic. Parliament ordered that a return be compiled of
diamond-drill expenditure, distinguishing districts. The
return when completed showed Ballarat district to be
easily ahead, Colonel Smith, one of the members, being at
the time in prominent opposition. It was put to Gillies
that Mr. (now Farthing) Fink, Ministerial M.P. for Mary-
borough, would hebraicalb' snort if he saw how his dis-
trict had been deserted for Radical Ballarat. Neither
Fink nor Parliament ever saw the return.

A London city paper is responsible for the state-
ment that there were such misstatements in the London-
derry prospectus as to warrant dissatisfied shareholders
in applying for a return of their money. The alleged mis-
statements are with reference to the area of the property
to be acquired, which has been erroneously given—not,
the Weekly Mixing Herald admits, with any fraudulent
intention.
Apropos a recent imposing Sydney cere-
mony, what a grotesque epithet is that of
“ brides,” as applied to the unfortunate girls who
immolate their youth and sex in a nunnery!
Davidson’s “ Ballad of a Nun ” should be writ
large in every breviary, and posted on the walls of
every convent in superstitiondom.
A newspaper-proprietor, lately offered an
Australian serial story for his “great weekly,”
said: “Who’ll read it? I don’t buy a pig in a
poke.” “ I suppose your editor could look through
it,” was the reasonable suggestion. “He has
plenty to do ; besides, there are not three editors
in Australia fit to judge a serial story in manu-
script. Every time it has been done for me I’ve
been sorry before the tale finished. No ; I prefer
taking my serial matter from syndicates, and not,
as you may think, because I get it cheaper, but
because I get it from people upon whose judgment
I can rely. Why don’t Australian authors get in
with the syndicates? I’d rather take yours than
the work of a stranger : but I must obtain it
through an experienced and approved source.”
There is only one N.S. Wales (licensed)
distillery, and that makes nun. But there are
hundreds of illicit distilleries.
In a Melbourne court, recently, a witness,
being examined to see if he was qualified to swear
on tile infected Bible, stated that lie didn’t know
where he would go when he died. Whereupon
David Gaunson got up and stated that he didn’t
know where he would go either. The Bulletin
now gets up to state that it also doesn’t know
where D. Gaunson will go. It has certain grave
suspicions, that is all.
Dear Bulletin, —Re the man who learnt
brewing from books, what is shallower than the
sneer at “book-knowledge?” What could one
man’s experience do, unassisted by the accumu-
lated experience of his fellows; and what are
books but the store-houses of that experience?
Individual theoretical knowledge should be sup-
plemented by individual practical experiment.
True —but what, after all, is individual theoretical
knowledge but the practical experience of many ?
—collected in books from all the world and all the
ages? The fallacy is akin to that which would
make “head-work” a thing apart. AH work is
head-work—the greater contains the less 1 Stun
the pick-and-shovel man with a crack on the
skull, and how much muck will he shift till the
head has resumed operations ? — Heretic,
Gregory Facing Richardson.
S. E. Gregory began real cricket with the
Oriental Club, one of the crack juniors. He was
then only 16, and was given a chance at the re-
commendation of Donnan, who thought well of
him as a bowler. The other members of the club
did nob expect much from the diminutive young-
ster, bub in his first match (against Double Bay
Club), Gregory, bowling slow leg-breaks, (“whir-
lies ”), took six wickets for 23. Playing with the
Orientals he developed his bowling and fielding,
but for a long time gave no promise of becoming a
great batsman. He joined the Sydney Club with
Donnan, became a remarkable field, and began
batting. Donnan, his mentor, was at this time
going great guns in interprovincial cricket-
had been playing it from ’87 —and in ’B9
had gained the premiership with an average
of 72. It was in ’89 —this year of Donnan’s—■
that Gregory first played interprovincially.
The ’9O team for England was being selected.
Ten men were chosen ; they voted unanimously
for Donnan. Ti.is was at night, when it was tov*
late to apprise Donnan by wire. In the morning,
the two members who were going to telegraph had
a confab, which resulted in wiring for Gregory.
The rest of the team were dumbfounded when
Gregory joined them on the ship for England.
He had been put in as a draw—he was a fair
bat, very little, and wonderful in the field.
Many people say it was Gregory’s English
trip that made him what he is—perhaps the
most dexterous of living batsmen. Gregory’s
luck has fallen upon an enterprising young
Sydney man who has dared to make bats
himself in competition with the oldest English
firms. The bat which Gregory holds is a present
from this Sydney maker. Gregory tried it in
practice, liked it exceedingly, scored 201 (not out)
with it, the first time he used it, in the match
against the combined country team. Donnan,
who used a bat by the same maker, in the same
match, also scored 201 (not out). Gregory has
gone on this season making wonderful scores with
the new bat : 201 (not out), 153 (not out), 87, 201
(in the first test-match). 101 against Melbourne,
when the rest of the team succumbed, 42, and
minor scores; in all, 855, giving an average of
85.5 per innings. Iredale, Callaway, Turner,
Bannerinan and Mackenzie have each a bat by the
same maker. Ha is Mr. A. Dye, of 41 Cleve-
land-street, Redfern, Sydney.

Judas: A Strike Incident .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Judas ! lloi'o ho comes—“ Judas, the
traitor !”
He passed among them with downeast head,
glancing fearfully from side to side at the
ominous, lowering faces. All reviled him; the
women spat upon him; several of the men
attempted to strike him. It was all the two
policemen could do to save him from the fury of
the crowd. Presently stones began to fly. Most
of them passed wide of the mark, but one of them
found its billet. He reeled as the missile struck
him, and then recovering himself, ,he broke from
his escort, and rushed wildly down the narrow
street, followed by a shower of stones and curses,
and the execrating shouts of “Judas 1 Judas 1”
• • •
It was the time of a great strike. The
strikers knew not the curse of their heritage as
they battled against starvation with ever-
dwindling strength.
Itwvas reserved for one man to bring it home to
them —one of their own number—a traitor—
Judas ! He was the first to fail, as he had been
the last to join them. In going back to the yoke
and pittance, he paved the way for others to
follow, one by one, until it seemed as if the"long
slow weeks of suffering and self-sacrifice had been
once more in vain.
Can you wonder that they hated him ? And
the Law of the land—the Law which put forth all
its powers to thrust them back into the abyss—
can you wonder that they hated that too, and
rebelled against it ?
And he, the traitor? Ah, those are not merely
the execrations of a handful of betrayed brethren
that greet him to-night: they are the voices of un-
born generations which send up, as lie slinks along,
that universal shout of “Judas ! Judas 1”
* * •
Later he stands hatless, beneath a lamp in
the deserted street. His faoe is covered with
blood where the stone struck him. He has had
more than one bitter experience this evening.
Soon after he escaped from his pursuers he stag-
gered, sick and faint, into a hotel. The bur was
filled with men, many of whom were strikers,
though he failed to notice them till it was too late
to retreat. He reeled to the counter.
“ Gave me some brandy, quick,” he said, “ for
the love of God ! ”
The bottle was passed to'him in silence. The
room had been filled with clamour when lie
entered ; now an ominous silence prevailed. He
would have given worlds to search the assemblage
for one pitying, relenting face ; but be dared not
lift his eyes. He hastily swallowed the spirit and
thrust a coin across the counter. Then a voice
was heard.
“ What, Hennelly, would you touch his cursed
silver—blood-money! ”
The publican hesitated. His sympathies were
with the men ou strike, yet lie could not help
feeling some faint sense of pity for the abject
wretch before him. But pity at times is a non-
p tying sentiment. He flicked the coin back
it 'am.
“ No,” he said, “he has had the drink; but I
will not touch his money ! ”
“Curse you!” cried the hunted wretch, as he
shivered theglass upon the floor. “Oh, curse you
all !’’ and he rushed Jrom the place, followed by
derisive laughter.
He is recalling the scene now, as he stands
beneath the street-lamp, with the rain pattering
upon his uncovered head.
“My God!” he cries, “I cannot stand this
much longer.
• # •
Later still. He is walking, he knows not
whither, although the street seems familiar. In
truth he is close to the factory. Suddenly he
hears a sound of sobbing. A child—a little boy—
is crouched against the fence, crying bitterly.
“ What is the matter?” he asks.
The child tells him—he is lost.
“ Come with me,” he says ; “ I will take you
home.” He knows the boy—for over twelve
months he worked alongside his father in the
factory.
The child gives his hand trustingly, and they go
on together. A little further on he stops to look
at his watch, and the boy gets a glimpse of his
face.
Instantly the child endeavours to wrest himself
free, at the same time crying out, in terror, “ Let
me go ! let me go 1”
“ What is the matter ?” he asks. “ Come with
me ; I will take you home.”
“No, no,” exclaims the child, beating frantically
the detaining hand, “not with you —I know you.
You are Judas!”
Instantly he releases the boy.
“ Even the children !” he groans. “ Oh, God, I
am heavily punished!”
* * »
He is at the door of his lodgings. Although
it is very late now, there is a faint light within.
He ope.ns the door softly and enters. As he is
passing up the passage, the door of the sitting-
room opens and his landlady calls him.
“ I have been waiting up for you, sir,” she says
as a beginning. “ I am very sorry to have to do
anything unpleasant ; you have been near two
years now in my house, sir, but I must give you
warning. You see, sir,” she went on, seeing that
be was about to speak, “ it’s the other boarders.
They’ve heard about you going back to the
factory, and to-night they all came to me and
said that thfey would not live in the same house
as a ‘scab,’ and that if you did not go to-night
they would all leave in the morning ; and so you
see, sir, I could do nothing else except to ask you,
althpugh I am sorry to lose you,,!if
“To-night!” he said, bitterly, “they are in a
hurry—to night ! ”
“If you please, sir, that’s what they said, and
if you could make it convenient to go, sir—.there
is that little matter of arrears, we would call that
square.”
“Very well,” he said, turning on his heel
abruptly, “I will think over it.”
“To-night!” he muttered, as ho lit the candle
in his own room ; “ to-night ! Ah well, as well
to-night as to-morrow, or the next day.”
« * •
In Hie morning a small crowd gathered in
the street, discussing the news.
“Poor fellow !” said a woe-begone woman who
was nursing a wan and woe-begone infant; “ poor
fellow ! ”
“ Fom pity him !” exclaimed a man at her side ;
“only for him we should have won. As it is—
look at me, look at yourself, look at the child,
half-starved now, and likely to be whole-starved
soon, thanks to him —and you pity him !”
And one by one the crowd took the question
up, and discussed it; for by this time the news
was generally known.
Judas, the traitor, had hanged himself 1
E. F. SQUIRES.
The late Randolph Churchill began to take
to drink directly after the malicious scandals
whispered about London five years ago re the
beautiful Lady Randolph and the late Duke of
Clarence. He has been going down-hill ever
since, and his excursions to Africa and America
were only undertaken in the hope that he would
pull up.

Dear Bulletin, —You think a J.P. should
“ pay for the honor.” Well, some of them do. I
know one elderly, honorary magistrate of rather
more than ordinary intelligence who is much in
request. Whenever the sergeant of police at a
township 13 miles away wires for hi,m he has to
start off so as to reach the place by 10 a.m. Being
a business man, he must either close his shop or
pay someone to look after it.for him. Then when
he reaches the township he is often-detained by
his magisterial duties till sor 6 p,m. He has to
pay hotel expenses for himself and a pair of horses,
and to risk a smash by driving home along an
imperfectly-cleared bush-track at night.— Justice.
As a general average, 8000 persons disap-
pear yearly in various parts of the British empire,
and are never seen or heard of again. This makes
no allowance for the class whose disappearances
pass unnoticed, because they have no friends ; it
deals only with people who vanish from their
homes, and are sought after more or less diligently,
yet concerning whom neither a footprint, nor an
echo, nor a conjecture is ever found. No spookist
ever raises the ghost of one of these persons, and
gets from him an account of how he stepped out
over the rim of the earth and fell into space, or
how he walked up into the atmosphere and didn’t
come down again, or how he turned the corner of
time and marched straight into eternity with his
boots on. It sets one thinking of the weird theory
of a “ disappearance disease,” which has no
symptoms,' or degrees, or outward signs, and
which, when it comes to a climax, simply abolishes
the patient and his clothes and boots and carpet-
bag, all in an instant, so that you see him for one
moment on a ladder and the next you see an empty
ladder and a falling paint-pot, and nothing more.
Meanwhile, by way of postscript, The Bulletin
cordially suggests that Parkes should disappear—
that he should go out of N.S.W. Assembly House
some sunny afternoon, just for a moment, and
vanish away at the door as utterly as if he had
never existed, leaving only a poem on the door-
step as a sign that he had been there. It would
be a romantic ending to the old man’s career. To
grow old and querulous and out-of-date is common-
place. To die in the ordinary fashion is still more
commonplace—everybody does it. But to evapo-
rate suddenly and utterly is novel and weird and
romantic. People have searched for Leichhardt’s
bones for half a century simply because he dis-
appeared, and they would hunt for Parkes’ bones,
in all probability, for twice as long. And, on the
other hand, if he dies in the ordinary way, inside
of five years nobody will remember that he ever
had any bones at all.
Editor Bulletin.— Sir, — Be your article
“A State Grog - Department.” I have been
both a licensed merchant and a brewer. The
general public have little idea of the wretched
stuff sold them in the name of wines, spirits and
beer. Sixty years ago you could buy good, still
peat-reek whisky for about Bs. a gallon ; now
you have to pay something like 255. for so-called
Scotch wfrisky which is, in many eases, neither
more nor less than raw grain or potato spirit kept
in sherry butts. Irish whisky never pretended to
be distilled from malt, but it is, on the
average, a purer spirit and generally well
matured. Rum is more easily adulterated per-
haps than any other liquor. One gallon of good
over proof West India rum, with the addition of
chemicals such as gum catechu, burnt sugar
and potato-spirit can, by manipulation, be made
into five gals. There are hundreds of ways of
adulterating brandy. Gin or geneva used to be
made of rye flavored with juniper-berries. Now
it is potato-spirit and turpentine. “ Londqn
gin ” is, on the average, perfect rot-gut. The ale
and beer served out is said to be made from
pure malt and hops. It is but rarely any-
thing of the kind. Sugar, or saccharin, made
from beets, turnips, and carrots, is largely used.
And “ hops -substitute ” takes the place of
hops. This is largely manufactured in England.
I was once agent for an English maker of this
material, which was used extensively in Liverpool
and the neighbourhood. Did you ever see the
stuff called “finings” that many of the brewers
put into the casks as they leave the brevrery ? It is
of the colour and consistency of dog’s-vomit, and
is made from hoofs, horns, and sinews of animals.
If you saw it put in you would never have a
“ long-sleever ” again.-— ZoziMUS (Tas.).
The Anarchist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dawn hangs heavy on the distant hill,
The darkness shudders slowly into light;
And from the weary bosom of the night
The pent winds sigh, then sink with horror still.
Naked and grey, the guillotine stands square
Upon the hill, while from its'base the crowd
Surges out far, and waits, to silence cowed.
Impatient for the thing to happen there.
Listen ! The bells within the tower toll
Five naked notes ; and down within his cell
The prisoner hears and mutters : “ It is well.”
Tho’, like that other knife, each cuts his soul.
His sick nerves from the probing echoes shrink.
“This is the end,” he says ; “ let me be strong ;
Let me be brave till then—’tis not for long;
I must not think of it—l must not think 1”
See, thro’ the courtyard guarded, comes the slight,
Thin figure of the anarchist. ■ Amazed
He sees the thousand faces swiftly raised;
The billows of the crowd break into white.
One narrow, alien glance below, and then
The scene fades dimly from his film-glazed eyes;
And, shuddering, he sees his past arise—
The cycle of his life begins again.
And, as misshapen memories crowd fast
Upon him, jostling in unholy strife,
Athwart the dull, drab level of his life
Stand sharply out the blood-stains of his past!
His youth, before he knew he had it, lost.
His father’s body by an accident
’Neath the rich man’s remorseless mill-wheels
pent—
A corpse ; and sister, mother, brother tossed
Out to the mercy of the merciless !
His stricken mother next, her humble niche
Was needed by the reckless and the rich ;
And death was easier than life’s loneliness.
His sister, she had fortune in her face—
She won it, too, till vice’s fingers tore
The freshness from her figure, and no more
In idleness she flaunted her disgrace.
He lost her, stifled in the world’s wide smother,
For years ; till one night on the street they met:
She seized him —he can feel that hot thrill yet !
She spoke him—knowing not he was her brother !
Wrong reeking of the rich incessantly !
Oppression and oppression o’er again !
Till from the smouldering hate within his brain
Mad fever fired the fuse of Anarchy !
Then plot and cunning, weak, futile and mean,
The maddened one against the many, thus—
He strove to strangle Order’s octopus—■
He gained the goal at last—the guillotine !
It waits him grim and grey ; he sees it not,
Nor hears the rising murmur ripple out
To the crowd’s edge, and, turning, die in doubt;
The vague, uncertain future threatens —what?
What, shall he speak, fling out his last reply?
Why waste the time in trivialities?
One throbbing thought now holds him ; and
there is
No room for sign or speech—he has to die !
Swift are the too-few preparations made ;
The block is waiting, waits the eager knife.
And for the last few minutes of his life
All facts but that life’s ending from him fade !
Only a murmur wavers up and shakes
The sullen air, then hesitates and dies.
And the grim hush of horror stifled lies,
Suspended like a billow ere it breaks.
One bitter prayer, half-curse, he mutters when
The knife hangs high abot'e, and the world
waits,
And e’er it swoops an age it hesitates ;
The word is given, breaths are drawn, and then—
With eyes and soul close shut (be swift, relief!)
The prisoner waits the end that does not come.
For hark! that heavy, low, tumultuous hum
That surges, surges till it shouts “Reprieve !’’
“ Reprieved and pardoned !” All his senses swim
In one rose-mist. As Sleep’s soft hand that
soothes
The tense, strained limbs of fevered day, and
smoothes
Life’s knotted nerves-so comes release to him.!
Refreshed he wakes. His weary soul*, set free,
Was flung out far, and for a minute’s space,
Had seen the sadness of God’s silent face —
The mighty calm of immortality.
How like a triumph his home-coming ! Then
The glorious news that mpthira, how that Right
Had routed Wrong, for ever faction’s figjit
Was finished, and the world was one !
Then swiftly, through his swimming, mist-dimmed
eyes,
He sees the good and great uprise again ;
And reason rings the knell of grief and pain;
The gladdened new world lapped in sunlight lies.
Long life was his with honor. On fame’s breath
His name was borne, until in perfect peace—
Glad like a mellow fruit to fall and cease—
His long life ripened richly into death.
But none knew this but he. The crowd still wai ts ;
Shoots swift the lightning of the knife, and loud
Roars the hoarse thunder from the sated crowd;
And justice has been done !
God compensates.
Dunedin, M.L.

Melb. Arous unearths the fact tjhat Barren
Carrington, when at - college, could drink a bottle
of Bordeaux in the shortest time on record. At a

fastest drinker took 15 secs. If Carrington is on
for another competition The Bulletin knows a
Chinaman who will sup a bottleful of wine out of
a bowl, with two bits of stick, and give the Barren
—also with two bits of stick—30 secs, start.
Stakes, .£1000. Money and Chinaman will be
produced at this office.
The Haunted House .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is no question that the house was
queer and weird and ghastly. Not only have I
seen it with my waking eyes, but in my dreams it
has come to me and mademe tremble. It was built of
ordinary rough red-pine boards and its foundations
consisted of silver-pine piles, driven deep into the
shingle bed of the creek that flowed directly
before the door. The creek-banks were steep, and
composed of walls of bluish-grey sandstone, 30ft.
high. The tall, straggling edifice clung to those
walls like the vine to its trellis, and reached the
top and towered one storey over the plateau,
across whieh wound the road. The surprise of
the traveller was extreme when an examination of
the place revealed a building 50ft. in height.
The rooms were as uncanny as the house.
Every room was a square. There were four
squares on each flat. In the floor of each room,
excepting those on the base, was a square aperture
just sufficient to allow an erect human figure to
pass. Each aperture had in its corner a rope reach-
ing to the floor below. These served for descent.
There were no staircases, but on each landing was
a portable ladder. Grooves and chocks fixed in
the floor made the ladder available for ascent.
Myriad half-inch cracks, as if economy in timber
had been a primary consideration, admitted day-
light through the floors, and in the wet season
transmitted rain vertically from room to room.
The owner of this eerie pile was mad as they
make them. None dared call upon him ; but when
the sun had set, the passing digger, pipe in mouth,
would pause to hearken to the dweller in the
abode of ghosts. Curious rumbles would be heard,
varied from time to time by hoarse shrieks, hor-
rible imprecations, and hideous, mocking laughter.
Suddenly the voice would be toned down to soft,
mellow, tremulous cadences ; these would be sup-
planted by wild gyrations that sent a thrill through
the listener akin to the shiver which appals
the passenger when the captain has wrecked the
ship. Also, there were swishing noises as of
bodies sliding down at reduced rates of friction,
and the heavier draggings of more cumbersome
materials; and interspersed with these weird sounds
were certain mighty bangs and crashes.
• # »
In the spring of ’73, a short man with a
fringe of white hair round his chin and other
appearances indicating an age of well-nigh sixty
years, advanced resolutely up the creek. His
movements were not sprightly, but there was a
sternness in his every motion not characteristic of
the aimless wayfarer.
“ And so,” soliloquised the traveller as he came
opposite the Haunted House, “ this is the resi-
dence of my mad brother.” Walking swiftly
over a long but very slightly graded plank he.
reached the lower door and knocked, but there was
no answer. He went through the rooms on the
ground floor. They were empty, but he heard a
thud overhead that made him wince. Catching
the ladder he gained the first flight.
Again that awful thud. Soon he was on the
second landing. Bending over one of the aper-
tures was the brother of 30 years ago engaged in
pushing an oil-drum through it. Down it went
and resounded on the floor below. A maniacal
laugh followed. The next instant the lunatic was
clasped in his brother’s arms.
» « »
“ Yes,” said the exultant madman, “ I
caught him—l killed him, the real murderer, the
scoundrel who got me transported 30 years ago for
the murder of the brewer at Coope’s. I put his
bones, after I had boiled him, into that'drum. I
flattened the projecting neck and riveted all
together. I built this place on purpose. For 20
years I have been happy dashing those bones from
room to room.”
“ My God ! ” exclaimed the other in tones of
horror, “you killed the wrong man—you have
dashed the bones of an inngcent person. The
real criminal was the husband of Isabel.”
The insane brother passed his hand over his
forehead, as if feeling for a tangible junction of
hair-roots and brain, and said, with curious logic :
“Doesn’t matter a damn—l wanted compensa-
tion—l thought her brother was the murderer,
apd that he blamed me to prevent our marriage.
Perhaps this brute was worse than the villain she
married ; who knows ?”
« # »
And when the district grew more settled,
the police, “ from information received,” arrested
two old crazy men in the haunted house.
Maoriland.
POLYPE.
Rev. S. B. Fellows, New Guinea mis-
sionary, in Australasian Methodist Missionary
Review
T.he native women here have also earned an unenviable
notoriety for impurity, and I had been told to expect a
greeting in filthy poetry taught them by some men with
white skins and black hearts. However, the presence of
my wife in my home has completely stopped anynmpure
advances towards the missionary.
How completely satisfactory ! But the New
Guinea climate being so trying to white women,
nearly all who go there find a yearly trip
South necessary, while th.eir husbands remain
tethered to the vineyard or cocoa-nut patch. The
noble male saints who have gone out with glad
tidings for the heathen are thus exposed to a sore
annual trial from which, through prayer, they no
doubt emerge triumphant. Another'bit; —
It is becoming quite the fashion for mothers to bring
their babies to the Mission House for inspection. I would
not like to reckon how many have been washed and oiled
on the verandah.
We are thinking of starting a Neglected Children’*
Fund, so as to purchase suitable condensed milk, &0., for
infants who are deprived of their natural sustenance.
“Feeding-bottle my lambs I” Admirable mis-
sionary !
Children over six month* of age could be kept for about
£2 a year —clothes included.
Note the true parsonic stress on clothes. Clothes
are the thin end of the wedge which splits up the
natural man, and opens the way for progress,
civilisation, phthisis, churches, stipends, the eol-
lection-plate, the sustentation fund, fat ministers
frolicking round at kiss-in-the-ring picnics, Heaven
and harps, Hell and sulphur, and everything else
worth having; and all for X 2 a year. Noble
Fellows !
